style="text-align:center"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:New_Horizons_-_Logo2_big.png"><img alt="New Horizons - Logo2 big.png" data-file-height="930" data-file-width="943" decoding="async" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/New_Horizons_-_Logo2_big.png/300px-New_Horizons_-_Logo2_big.png 2x" width="150"/></a>\n<div class="" style="width:100%; text-align:center; border-top:1px #aaa solid"><b><a href="/wiki/New_Frontiers_program" title="New Frontiers program">New Frontiers program</a></b></div><div style="width:100%"><div class="noprint" style="float: right; text-align:right;padding-left:0.5em;"><i><a href="/wiki/Juno_(spacecraft)" title="Juno (spacecraft)">Juno</a></i>&#160;&#8594;</div></div>&#160;</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><i><b>New Horizons</b></i> is an <a href="/wiki/Interplanetary_spaceflight" title="Interplanetary spaceflight">interplanetary</a> <a href="/wiki/Space_probe" title="Space probe">space probe</a> that was launched as a part of <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/New_Frontiers_program" title="New Frontiers program">New Frontiers program</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20150718_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20150718-3">[3]</a></sup> Engineered by the <a href="/wiki/Johns_Hopkins_University" title="Johns Hopkins University">Johns Hopkins University</a> <a href="/wiki/Applied_Physics_Laboratory" title="Applied Physics Laboratory">Applied Physics Laboratory</a> (APL) and the <a href="/wiki/Southwest_Research_Institute" title="Southwest Research Institute">Southwest Research Institute</a> (SwRI), with a team led by <a href="/wiki/Alan_Stern" title="Alan Stern">S. Alan Stern</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tri_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-tri-4">[4]</a></sup> the spacecraft was launched in 2006 with the primary mission to perform a <a href="/wiki/Planetary_flyby" title="Planetary flyby">flyby</a> study of the <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a> system in 2015, and a secondary mission to fly by and study one or more other <a href="/wiki/Kuiper_belt" title="Kuiper belt">Kuiper belt</a> objects (KBOs) in the decade to follow, which as of 2019 includes <span class="nowrap"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2014_MU69" title="2014 MU69">2014 MU<span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;"><span style="font-size:80%;">69</span></span></a></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nhtp_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-nhtp-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20150713_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20150713-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20150706_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20150706-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20150706-db_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20150706-db-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20150828_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20150828-9">[9]</a></sup> It is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_artificial_objects_leaving_the_Solar_System" title="List of artificial objects leaving the Solar System">fifth space probe</a> to achieve the <a href="/wiki/Escape_velocity" title="Escape velocity">escape velocity</a> needed to leave the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>.\n</p><p>On January 19, 2006, <i>New Horizons</i> was launched from <a href="/wiki/Cape_Canaveral_Air_Force_Station" title="Cape Canaveral Air Force Station">Cape Canaveral Air Force Station</a> by an <a href="/wiki/Atlas_V" title="Atlas V">Atlas V</a> <a href="/wiki/Rocket" title="Rocket">rocket</a> directly into an Earth-and-solar <a href="/wiki/Escape_velocity" title="Escape velocity">escape trajectory</a> with a speed of about 16.26&#160;km/s (10.10&#160;mi/s; 58,500&#160;km/h; 36,400&#160;mph). It was the fastest man-made object ever launched from Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-APL-20070116_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-APL-20070116-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sciam20130225_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-sciam20130225-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ionine20150609_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-ionine20150609-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ET-20171213_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-ET-20171213-13">[13]</a></sup> After a brief encounter with <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroid</a> <a href="/wiki/132524_APL" title="132524 APL">132524 APL</a>, <i>New Horizons</i> proceeded to <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>, making its closest approach on February 28, 2007, at a distance of 2.3&#160;million kilometers (1.4&#160;million miles). The Jupiter flyby provided a <a href="/wiki/Gravity_assist" title="Gravity assist">gravity assist</a> that increased <i>New Horizons</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'</span> speed; the flyby also enabled a general test of <i>New Horizons</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'</span> scientific capabilities, returning data about <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Jupiter" title="Atmosphere of Jupiter">the planet\'s atmosphere</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Jupiter" title="Moons of Jupiter">moons</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere_of_Jupiter" title="Magnetosphere of Jupiter">magnetosphere</a>.\n</p><p>Most of the post-Jupiter voyage was spent in <a href="/wiki/Hibernation_(spacecraft)" title="Hibernation (spacecraft)">hibernation mode</a> to preserve on-board systems, except for brief annual checkouts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> On December 6, 2014, <i>New Horizons</i> was brought back online for the Pluto encounter, and instrument check-out began.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> On January 15, 2015, the spacecraft began its approach phase to Pluto.\n</p><p>On July 14, 2015, at 11:49&#160;<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UTC" title="UTC">UTC</a>, it flew 12,500&#160;km (7,800&#160;mi) above the surface of Pluto,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20150714-kc_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20150714-kc-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AP-20150714_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-AP-20150714-17">[17]</a></sup> making it the first spacecraft to explore the <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_planet" title="Dwarf planet">dwarf planet</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20150706-db_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20150706-db-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20150714-kn_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20150714-kn-18">[18]</a></sup> On October 25, 2016, at 21:48&#160;UTC, the last of the recorded data from the Pluto flyby was received from <i>New Horizons</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20161028_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20161028-19">[19]</a></sup> Having completed its flyby of Pluto,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20151211-rj_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20151211-rj-20">[20]</a></sup> <i>New Horizons</i> then maneuvered for a flyby of Kuiper belt object <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/(486958)_2014_MU69" title="(486958) 2014 MU69">(486958) 2014 MU<span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;"><span style="font-size:80%;">69</span></span></a></span> "Ultima Thule",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20150828-tt_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20150828-tt-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SP-20150828_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-SP-20150828-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AP-20151022_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-AP-20151022-23">[23]</a></sup> which occurred on January 1, 2019,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20190210_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20190210-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20181231-kc_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20181231-kc-25">[25]</a></sup> when it was 43.4&#160;<a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">AU</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20150828-tt_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20150828-tt-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SP-20150828_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-SP-20150828-22">[22]</a></sup> In August 2018, NASA cited results by <i><a href="#Alice">Alice</a></i> on <i>New Horizons</i> to confirm the existence of a "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrogen_wall" title="Hydrogen wall">hydrogen wall</a>" at the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System#Farthest_regions" title="Solar System">outer edges of the Solar System</a>. This "wall" was first detected in 1992 by the two <a href="/wiki/Voyager_program" title="Voyager program">Voyager spacecraft</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GRL-20180807_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-GRL-20180807-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LS-20180809_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-LS-20180809-27">[27]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div style="clear:left;"></div>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2>Contents</h2><span launch</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-54"><a href="#Videos"><span class="tocnumber">13.2</span> <span class="toctext">Videos</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-55"><a href="#Timeline"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">Timeline</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-56"><a href="#Preparation_phase"><span class="tocnumber">14.1</span> <span class="toctext">Preparation phase</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-57"><a href="#Launch_phase"><span class="tocnumber">14.2</span> <span class="toctext">Launch phase</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-58"><a href="#Jupiter_pre-encounter_phase"><span class="tocnumber">14.3</span> <span class="toctext">Jupiter pre-encounter phase</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-59"><a href="#Jupiter_encounter_phase"><span class="tocnumber">14.4</span> <span class="toctext">Jupiter encounter phase</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-60"><a href="#Pluto_pre-encounter_phase"><span class="tocnumber">14.5</span> <span class="toctext">Pluto pre-encounter phase</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-61"><a href="#Pluto_Science_phase"><span class="tocnumber">14.6</span> <span class="toctext">Pluto Science phase</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-62"><a href="#(486958)_2014_MU69_pre-encounter_phase"><span class="tocnumber">14.7</span> <span class="toctext"><span>(486958) 2014 MU<sub>69</sub></span> pre-encounter phase</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-63"><a href="#2014_MU69_Science_phase_and_beyond"><span class="tocnumber">14.8</span> <span class="toctext"><span>2014 MU<sub>69</sub></span> Science phase and beyond</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-64"><a href="#Post-mission_phase"><span class="tocnumber">14.9</span> <span class="toctext">Post-mission phase</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-65"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">15</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-66"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">16</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-67"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">17</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-68"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">18</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-69"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">19</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Exploration_of_Pluto" title="Exploration of Pluto">Exploration of Pluto</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:USPS_Pluto_Stamp_-_October_1991.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="191" data-file-width="300" decoding="async" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/5a/USPS_Pluto_Stamp_-_October_1991.jpg/220px-USPS_Pluto_Stamp_-_October_1991.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/5/5a/USPS_Pluto_Stamp_-_October_1991.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:USPS_Pluto_Stamp_-_October_1991.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USPS" title="USPS">USPS</a> stamp issued in 1991 that served as motivation for planetary scientists to send a probe to Pluto</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:New_horizons_(NASA).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="955" data-file-width="1206" decoding="async" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/New_horizons_%28NASA%29.jpg/220px-New_horizons_%28NASA%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/New_horizons_%28NASA%29.jpg/330px-New_horizons_%28NASA%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/New_horizons_%28NASA%29.jpg/440px-New_horizons_%28NASA%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:New_horizons_(NASA).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Early concept art of the <i>New Horizons</i> spacecraft. The mission, led by the <a href="/wiki/Applied_Physics_Laboratory" title="Applied Physics Laboratory">Applied Physics Laboratory</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alan_Stern" title="Alan Stern">Alan Stern</a>, eventually became the first mission to Pluto.</div></div></div>\n<p>In August 1992, <a href="/wiki/Jet_Propulsion_Laboratory" title="Jet Propulsion Laboratory">JPL</a> scientist Robert Staehle called Pluto discoverer <a href="/wiki/Clyde_Tombaugh" title="Clyde Tombaugh">Clyde Tombaugh</a>, requesting permission to visit his planet. "I told him he was welcome to it," Tombaugh later remembered, "though he\'s got to go one long, cold trip."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sobel1993_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sobel1993-28">[28]</a></sup> The call eventually led to a series of proposed Pluto missions, leading up to <i>New Horizons</i>.\n</p><p>Stamatios "Tom" Krimigis, head of the <a href="/wiki/Applied_Physics_Laboratory" title="Applied Physics Laboratory">Applied Physics Laboratory</a>\'s space division, one of many entrants in the New Frontiers Program competition, formed the <i>New Horizons</i> team with Alan Stern in December 2000.  Appointed as the project\'s <a href="/wiki/Principal_investigator" title="Principal investigator">principal investigator</a>, Stern was described by Krimigis as "the personification of the Pluto mission".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alan-stern_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-alan-stern-29">[29]</a></sup> <i>New Horizons</i> was based largely on Stern\'s work since <i>Pluto 350</i> and involved most of the team from <i>Pluto Kuiper Express</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newhorizonsbook_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-newhorizonsbook-30">[30]</a></sup> The <i>New Horizons</i> proposal was one of five that were officially submitted to NASA. It was later selected as one of two finalists to be subject to a three-month concept study, in June 2001. The other finalist, POSSE (Pluto and Outer Solar System Explorer), was a separate, but similar Pluto mission concept by the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Colorado_Boulder" title="University of Colorado Boulder">University of Colorado Boulder</a>, led by principal investigator <a href="/wiki/Larry_W._Esposito" title="Larry W. Esposito">Larry W. Esposito</a>, and supported by the JPL, <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_Martin" title="Lockheed Martin">Lockheed Martin</a> and the <a href="/wiki/University_of_California" title="University of California">University of California</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-new-frontiers-finalists_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-new-frontiers-finalists-31">[31]</a></sup> However, the APL, in addition to being supported by <i>Pluto Kuiper Express</i> developers at the Goddard Space Flight Center and <a href="/wiki/Stanford_University" title="Stanford University">Stanford University</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-new-frontiers-finalists_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-new-frontiers-finalists-31">[31]</a></sup> were at an advantage; they had recently developed <i><a href="/wiki/NEAR_Shoemaker" title="NEAR Shoemaker">NEAR Shoemaker</a></i> for NASA, which had successfully entered orbit around <a href="/wiki/433_Eros" title="433 Eros">433 Eros</a> earlier in the year, and would later land on the asteroid to scientific and engineering fanfare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In November 2001, <i>New Horizons</i> was officially selected for funding as part of the New Frontiers program.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> However, the new NASA Administrator appointed by the <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_George_W._Bush" title="Presidency of George W. Bush">Bush Administration</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sean_O%27Keefe" title="Sean O\'Keefe">Sean O\'Keefe</a>, was not supportive of <i>New Horizons</i>, and effectively cancelled it by not including it in NASA\'s budget for 2003. NASA\'s Associate Administrator for the Science Mission Directorate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ed_Weiler" title="Ed Weiler">Ed Weiler</a> prompted Stern to lobby for the funding of <i>New Horizons</i> in hopes of the mission appearing in the <a href="/wiki/Planetary_Science_Decadal_Survey" title="Planetary Science Decadal Survey">Planetary Science Decadal Survey</a>; a prioritized "wish list", compiled by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_National_Research_Council" title="United States National Research Council">United States National Research Council</a>, that reflects the opinions of the scientific community. After an intense campaign to gain support for <i>New Horizons</i>, the Planetary Science Decadal Survey of 2003&#8211;2013 was published in the summer of 2002. <i>New Horizons</i> topped the list of projects considered the highest priority among the scientific community in the medium-size category; ahead of missions to the Moon, and even Jupiter. Weiler stated that it was a result that "[his] administration was not going to fight".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alan-stern_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-alan-stern-29">[29]</a></sup> Funding for the mission was finally secured following the publication of the report, and Stern\'s team were finally able to start building the spacecraft and its instruments, with a planned launch in January 2006 and arrival at Pluto in 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alan-stern_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-alan-stern-29">[29]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Alice_Bowman" title="Alice Bowman">Alice Bowman</a> became Mission Operations Manager.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mission_profile">Mission profile</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Mission profile">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:15-011a-NewHorizons-PlutoFlyby-ArtistConcept-14July2015-20150115.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1238" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="210" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/15-011a-NewHorizons-PlutoFlyby-ArtistConcept-14July2015-20150115.jpg/170px-15-011a-NewHorizons-PlutoFlyby-ArtistConcept-14July2015-20150115.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/15-011a-NewHorizons-PlutoFlyby-ArtistConcept-14July2015-20150115.jpg/255px-15-011a-NewHorizons-PlutoFlyby-ArtistConcept-14July2015-20150115.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/15-011a-NewHorizons-PlutoFlyby-ArtistConcept-14July2015-20150115.jpg/340px-15-011a-NewHorizons-PlutoFlyby-ArtistConcept-14July2015-20150115.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:15-011a-NewHorizons-PlutoFlyby-ArtistConcept-14July2015-20150115.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Artist\'s impression of <i>New Horizons</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'</span> <a href="#Pluto_system_encounter">close encounter with the Plutonian system</a></div></div></div>\n<p><i>New Horizons</i> is the first mission in NASA\'s New Frontiers mission category, larger and more expensive than the Discovery missions but smaller than the Flagship Program. The cost of the mission (including spacecraft and instrument development, launch vehicle, mission operations, data analysis, and education/public outreach) is approximately $700&#160;million over 15 years (2001&#8211;2016).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> The spacecraft was built primarily by <a href="/wiki/Southwest_Research_Institute" title="Southwest Research Institute">Southwest Research Institute</a> (SwRI) and the <a href="/wiki/Johns_Hopkins_University" title="Johns Hopkins University">Johns Hopkins</a> Applied Physics Laboratory. The mission\'s principal investigator is Alan Stern of the Southwest Research Institute (formerly NASA Associate Administrator).\n</p><p>After separation from the launch vehicle, overall control was taken by Mission Operations Center (MOC) at the Applied Physics Laboratory in <a href="/wiki/Howard_County,_Maryland" title="Howard County, Maryland">Howard County, Maryland</a>. The science instruments are operated at Clyde Tombaugh Science Operations Center (T-SOC) in <a href="/wiki/Boulder,_Colorado" title="Boulder, Colorado">Boulder, Colorado</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DoSS_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-DoSS-36">[36]</a></sup> Navigation is performed at various contractor facilities, whereas the navigational positional data and related celestial reference frames are provided by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Naval_Observatory_Flagstaff_Station" title="United States Naval Observatory Flagstaff Station">Naval Observatory Flagstaff Station</a> through Headquarters NASA and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/JPL" title="JPL">JPL</a>; <a href="/wiki/KinetX" title="KinetX">KinetX</a> is the lead on the <i>New Horizons</i> navigation team and is responsible for planning trajectory adjustments as the spacecraft speeds toward <a href="/wiki/Solar_System#Trans-Neptunian_region" title="Solar System">the outer Solar System</a>. Coincidentally the Naval Observatory Flagstaff Station was where the photographic plates were taken for the discovery of Pluto\'s moon <a href="/wiki/Charon_(moon)" title="Charon (moon)">Charon</a>; and the Naval Observatory is itself not far from the <a href="/wiki/Lowell_Observatory" title="Lowell Observatory">Lowell Observatory</a> where Pluto was discovered.\n</p><p><i>New Horizons</i> was originally planned as a voyage to the only unexplored planet in the Solar<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>System. When the spacecraft was launched, Pluto was still classified as a <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planet</a>, later to be <a href="/wiki/IAU_definition_of_planet" title="IAU definition of planet">reclassified</a> as a dwarf planet by the <a href="/wiki/International_Astronomical_Union" title="International Astronomical Union">International Astronomical Union</a> (IAU). Some members of the <i>New Horizons</i> team, including Alan Stern, disagree with the IAU definition and still describe Pluto as the ninth planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> Pluto\'s satellites <a href="/wiki/Nix_(moon)" title="Nix (moon)">Nix</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hydra_(moon)" title="Hydra (moon)">Hydra</a> also have a connection with the spacecraft: the first letters of their names (N and H) are the initials of <i>New Horizons</i>. The moons\' discoverers chose these names for this reason, plus Nix and Hydra\'s relationship to the mythological <a href="/wiki/Pluto_(mythology)" title="Pluto (mythology)">Pluto</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In addition to the science equipment, there are several cultural artifacts traveling with the spacecraft. These include a collection of 434,738 names stored on a compact disc,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> a piece of <a href="/wiki/Scaled_Composites" title="Scaled Composites">Scaled Composites</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/SpaceShipOne" title="SpaceShipOne">SpaceShipOne</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> a "Not Yet Explored" USPS stamp,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stamp-betz_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-stamp-betz-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stamp-070715_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-stamp-070715-42">[42]</a></sup> and a <a href="/wiki/Flag_of_the_United_States" title="Flag of the United States">Flag of the United States</a>, along with other mementos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>\n</p><p>About 30 grams (1&#160;oz) of Clyde Tombaugh\'s ashes are aboard the spacecraft, to commemorate his discovery of Pluto in 1930.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> A Florida-<a href="/wiki/50_State_Quarters" title="50 State Quarters">state quarter</a> coin, whose design commemorates human exploration, is included, officially as a trim weight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> One of the science packages (a dust counter) is named after <a href="/wiki/Venetia_Burney" title="Venetia Burney">Venetia Burney</a>, who, as a child, suggested the name "Pluto" after its discovery.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Goal">Goal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Goal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:15-150-NasaTeam-NewHorizonsCallsHomeAfterPlutoFlyby-20150714.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2565" data-file-width="4806" decoding="async" height="117" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/15-150-NasaTeam-NewHorizonsCallsHomeAfterPlutoFlyby-20150714.jpg/220px-15-150-NasaTeam-NewHorizonsCallsHomeAfterPlutoFlyby-20150714.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/15-150-NasaTeam-NewHorizonsCallsHomeAfterPlutoFlyby-20150714.jpg/330px-15-150-NasaTeam-NewHorizonsCallsHomeAfterPlutoFlyby-20150714.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/15-150-NasaTeam-NewHorizonsCallsHomeAfterPlutoFlyby-20150714.jpg/440px-15-150-NasaTeam-NewHorizonsCallsHomeAfterPlutoFlyby-20150714.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:15-150-NasaTeam-NewHorizonsCallsHomeAfterPlutoFlyby-20150714.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>View of Mission Operations at the <a href="/wiki/Applied_Physics_Laboratory" title="Applied Physics Laboratory">Applied Physics Laboratory</a> in <a href="/wiki/Laurel,_Maryland" title="Laurel, Maryland">Laurel, Maryland</a> (July 14, 2015)</div></div></div>\n<p>The goal of the mission is to understand the formation of the Plutonian system, the Kuiper belt, and the transformation of the early Solar System.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> The spacecraft collected data on the atmospheres, surfaces, interiors, and environments of Pluto and its moons. It will also study other objects in the Kuiper belt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> "By way of comparison, <i>New Horizons</i> gathered 5,000 times as much data at Pluto as <a href="/wiki/Mariner_program" title="Mariner program">Mariner</a> did at the <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Red Planet</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Some of the questions the mission attempts to answer are: What is Pluto\'s atmosphere made of and how does it behave? What does its surface look like? Are there large geological structures? How do <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a> particles interact with Pluto\'s atmosphere?<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Specifically, the mission\'s science objectives are to:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li>map the surface composition of Pluto and <a href="/wiki/Charon_(moon)" title="Charon (moon)">Charon</a></li>\n<li>characterize the geology and morphology of Pluto and Charon</li>\n<li>characterize the neutral <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Pluto" title="Atmosphere of Pluto">atmosphere of Pluto</a> and its escape rate</li>\n<li>search for an atmosphere around Charon</li>\n<li>map surface temperatures on Pluto and Charon</li>\n<li>search for rings and additional satellites around Pluto</li>\n<li>conduct similar investigations of one or more <a href="/wiki/Kuiper_belt" title="Kuiper belt">Kuiper belt</a> objects</li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Design_and_construction">Design and construction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Design and construction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><span class="mw-3d-wrapper" data-label="3D"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:New_Horizons.stl"><img alt="Interactive 3D model of New Horizons" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2880" data-file-width="5120" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/New_Horizons.stl/220px-New_Horizons.stl.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/New_Horizons.stl/330px-New_Horizons.stl.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/New_Horizons.stl/440px-New_Horizons.stl.png 2x" width="220"/></a></span> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:New_Horizons.stl" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Interactive 3D model of <i>New Horizons</i></div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spacecraft_subsystems">Spacecraft subsystems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Spacecraft subsystems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:New_Horizons_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1936" data-file-width="2552" decoding="async" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/New_Horizons_1.jpg/220px-New_Horizons_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/New_Horizons_1.jpg/330px-New_Horizons_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/New_Horizons_1.jpg/440px-New_Horizons_1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:New_Horizons_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>New Horizons</i> at <a href="/wiki/Kennedy_Space_Center" title="Kennedy Space Center">Kennedy Space Center</a> in 2005</div></div></div>\n<p>The spacecraft is comparable in size and general shape to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grand_piano" title="Grand piano">grand piano</a> and has been compared to a piano glued to a cocktail bar-sized satellite dish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore-2010_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Moore-2010-52">[52]</a></sup> As a point of departure, the team took inspiration from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ulysses_probe" title="Ulysses probe">Ulysses</a> spacecraft,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> which also carried a <a href="/wiki/Radioisotope_thermoelectric_generator" title="Radioisotope thermoelectric generator">radioisotope thermoelectric generator</a> (RTG) and dish on a box-in-box structure through the outer Solar System. Many subsystems and components have flight heritage from APL\'s <a href="/wiki/CONTOUR" title="CONTOUR">CONTOUR</a> spacecraft, which in turn had heritage from APL\'s <a href="/wiki/TIMED" title="TIMED">TIMED</a> spacecraft.\n</p><p><i>New Horizons</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'</span> body forms a triangle, almost 0.76&#160;m (2.5&#160;ft) thick. (The <i>Pioneers</i> have <a href="/wiki/Hexagon" title="Hexagon">hexagonal</a> bodies, whereas the <a href="/wiki/Voyager_program" title="Voyager program"><i>Voyagers</i></a>, <i>Galileo</i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens" title="Cassini&#8211;Huygens">Cassini&#8211;Huygens</a></i> have <a href="/wiki/Decagon" title="Decagon">decagonal</a>, hollow bodies.) A <a href="/wiki/7075_aluminium_alloy" title="7075 aluminium alloy">7075 aluminium alloy</a> tube forms the main structural column, between the launch vehicle adapter ring at the "rear", and the 2.1&#160;m (6&#160;ft 11&#160;in) radio <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dish_antenna" title="Dish antenna">dish antenna</a> affixed to the "front" flat side. The <a href="/wiki/Titanium" title="Titanium">titanium</a> fuel tank is in this tube. The RTG attaches with a 4-sided titanium mount resembling a gray pyramid or stepstool. Titanium provides strength and thermal isolation. The rest of the triangle is primarily sandwich panels of thin aluminium facesheet (less than <span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>&#8260;<sub>64</sub></span>&#160;in or 0.40&#160;mm) bonded to aluminium honeycomb core. The structure is larger than strictly necessary, with empty space inside. The structure is designed to act as <a href="/wiki/Radiation_hardening" title="Radiation hardening">shielding</a>, reducing electronics <a href="/wiki/Single_event_upset" title="Single event upset">errors caused by radiation</a> from the RTG. Also, the mass distribution required for a spinning spacecraft demands a wider triangle.\n</p><p>The interior structure is painted black to equalize temperature by <a href="/wiki/Thermal_radiation" title="Thermal radiation">radiative</a> heat transfer. Overall, the spacecraft is thoroughly blanketed to retain heat. Unlike the <i>Pioneers</i> and <i>Voyagers</i>, the radio dish is also enclosed in blankets that extend to the body. The heat from the RTG adds warmth to the spacecraft while it is in the outer Solar System. While in the inner Solar System, the spacecraft must prevent overheating, hence electronic activity is limited, power is diverted to <a href="/wiki/Shunt_(electrical)" title="Shunt (electrical)">shunts</a> with attached radiators, and <a href="/wiki/Louver" title="Louver">louvers</a> are opened to radiate excess heat. While the spacecraft is cruising inactively in the cold outer Solar System, the louvers are closed, and the shunt regulator reroutes power to electric <a href="/wiki/Resistor" title="Resistor">heaters</a>.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Propulsion_and_attitude_control">Propulsion and attitude control</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Propulsion and attitude control">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p><i>New Horizons</i> has both spin-stabilized (cruise) and three-axis stabilized (science) modes controlled entirely with <a href="/wiki/Hydrazine" title="Hydrazine">hydrazine</a> <a href="/wiki/Monopropellant_rocket" title="Monopropellant rocket">monopropellant</a>. Additional post launch <a href="/wiki/Delta-v" title="Delta-v">delta-v</a> of over 290&#160;m/s (1,000&#160;km/h; 650&#160;mph) is provided by a 77&#160;kg (170&#160;lb) internal tank. Helium is used as a pressurant, with an <a href="/wiki/Elastomer" title="Elastomer">elastomeric</a> diaphragm assisting expulsion. The spacecraft\'s on-orbit mass including fuel is over 470&#160;kg (1,040&#160;lb) on the Jupiter flyby trajectory, but would have been only 445&#160;kg (981&#160;lb) for the backup direct flight option to Pluto. Significantly, had the backup option been taken, this would have meant less fuel for later Kuiper belt operations.\n</p><p>There are 16 <a href="/wiki/Reaction_control_system" title="Reaction control system">thrusters</a> on <i>New Horizons</i>: four 4.4&#160;<a href="/wiki/Newton_(unit)" title="Newton (unit)">N</a> (1.0&#160;<a href="/wiki/Pound_(force)" title="Pound (force)">lbf</a>) and twelve 0.9&#160;N (0.2&#160;lbf) plumbed into redundant branches. The larger thrusters are used primarily for trajectory corrections, and the small ones (previously used on <i>Cassini</i> and the <i>Voyager</i> spacecraft) are used primarily for <a href="/wiki/Attitude_control" title="Attitude control">attitude control</a> and spinup/spindown maneuvers. Two star cameras are used to measure the spacecraft attitude. They are mounted on the face of the spacecraft and provide attitude information while in spin-stabilized or 3-axis mode. In between the time of star camera readings, spacecraft orientation is provided by dual redundant <a href="/wiki/Miniature_inertial_measurement_unit" title="Miniature inertial measurement unit">miniature inertial measurement units</a>. Each unit contains three solid-state <a href="/wiki/Gyroscope" title="Gyroscope">gyroscopes</a> and three <a href="/wiki/Accelerometer" title="Accelerometer">accelerometers</a>. Two Adcole <a href="/wiki/Attitude_control#Sun_sensor" title="Attitude control">Sun sensors</a> provide attitude determination. One detects the angle to the Sun, whereas the other measures spin rate and clocking.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Power">Power</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Power">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RTG_and_New_Horizons_in_background.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3008" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/RTG_and_New_Horizons_in_background.jpg/170px-RTG_and_New_Horizons_in_background.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/RTG_and_New_Horizons_in_background.jpg/255px-RTG_and_New_Horizons_in_background.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/RTG_and_New_Horizons_in_background.jpg/340px-RTG_and_New_Horizons_in_background.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:RTG_and_New_Horizons_in_background.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>New Horizons</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'</span> <a href="/wiki/Radioisotope_thermoelectric_generator" title="Radioisotope thermoelectric generator">RTG</a></div></div></div>\n<p>A cylindrical <a href="/wiki/Radioisotope_thermoelectric_generator" title="Radioisotope thermoelectric generator">radioisotope thermoelectric generator</a> (RTG) protrudes in the plane of the triangle from one vertex of the triangle. The RTG provided <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7002245700000000000&#9824;</span>245.7&#160;<a href="/wiki/Watt" title="Watt">W</a></span> of power at launch, and was predicted to drop approximately <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7000350000000000000&#9824;</span>3.5&#160;<a href="/wiki/Watt" title="Watt">W</a></span> every year, decaying to <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7002202000000000000&#9824;</span>202&#160;W</span> by the time of its encounter with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plutonian_system" title="Plutonian system">Plutonian system</a> in 2015 and will decay too far to power the transmitters in the 2030s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tri_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-tri-4">[4]</a></sup> There are no onboard batteries since RTG output is predictable, and load transients are handled by a capacitor bank and fast circuit breakers. As of January 2019, the power output of the RTG is about <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7002190000000000000&#9824;</span>190&#160;<a href="/wiki/Watt" title="Watt">W</a></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The RTG, model "<a href="/wiki/GPHS-RTG" title="GPHS-RTG">GPHS-RTG</a>", was originally a spare from the <i>Cassini</i> mission. The RTG contains 9.75&#160;kg (21.5&#160;lb) of <a href="/wiki/Plutonium-238" title="Plutonium-238">plutonium-238</a> oxide pellets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newhorizonsbook_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-newhorizonsbook-30">[30]</a></sup> Each pellet is clad in <a href="/wiki/Iridium" title="Iridium">iridium</a>, then encased in a graphite shell. It was developed by the U.S. <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Energy" title="United States Department of Energy">Department of Energy</a> at the Materials and Fuels Complex, a part of the <a href="/wiki/Idaho_National_Laboratory" title="Idaho National Laboratory">Idaho National Laboratory</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>\nThe original RTG design called for  10.9&#160;kg (24&#160;lb) of plutonium, but a unit less powerful than the original design goal was produced because of delays at the United States Department of Energy, including security activities, that delayed plutonium production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-radioshow_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-radioshow-56">[56]</a></sup> The mission parameters and observation sequence had to be modified for the reduced wattage; still, not all instruments can operate simultaneously. The Department of Energy transferred the space battery program from Ohio to Argonne in 2002 because of security concerns.\n</p><p>The amount of radioactive plutonium in the RTG is about one-third the amount on board the Cassini&#8211;Huygens probe when it launched in 1997. That Cassini launch was protested by some. The United States Department of Energy estimated the chances of a launch accident that would release radiation into the atmosphere at 1 in 350, and monitored the launch<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> as it always does when RTGs are involved. It was estimated that a worst-case scenario of total dispersal of on-board plutonium would spread the equivalent radiation of 80% the average annual dosage in North America from background radiation over an area with a radius of 105&#160;km (65&#160;mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Flight_computer">Flight computer</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Flight computer">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The spacecraft carries two <a href="/wiki/Computer" title="Computer">computer</a> systems: the Command and Data Handling system and the Guidance and Control processor. Each of the two systems is duplicated for <a href="/wiki/Redundancy_(engineering)" title="Redundancy (engineering)">redundancy</a>, for a total of four computers. The processor used for its flight computers is the <a href="/wiki/Mongoose-V" title="Mongoose-V">Mongoose-V</a>, a 12 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megahertz" title="Megahertz">MHz</a> radiation-hardened version of the <a href="/wiki/R3000" title="R3000">MIPS R3000</a> <a href="/wiki/Central_processing_unit" title="Central processing unit">CPU</a>. Multiple redundant clocks and timing routines are implemented in hardware and software to help prevent faults and downtime. To conserve heat and mass, spacecraft and instrument electronics are housed together in IEMs (integrated electronics modules). There are two redundant IEMs. Including other functions such as instrument and radio electronics, each IEM contains 9<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>boards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> The software of the probe runs on <a href="/wiki/Nucleus_RTOS" title="Nucleus RTOS">Nucleus RTOS</a> operating system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>\n</p><p>There have been two "safing" events, that sent the spacecraft into <a href="/wiki/Safe_mode_(spacecraft)" title="Safe mode (spacecraft)">safe mode</a>:\n</p>\n<ul><li>On March 19, 2007, the Command and Data Handling computer experienced an uncorrectable memory error and rebooted itself, causing the spacecraft to go into safe mode. The craft fully recovered within two days, with some data loss on Jupiter\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetotail" title="Magnetotail">magnetotail</a>. No impact on the subsequent mission was expected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></li>\n<li>On July 4, 2015, there was a CPU safing event caused by over-assignment of commanded science operations on the craft\'s approach to Pluto. Fortunately, the craft was able to recover within two days without major impacts on its mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Telecommunications_and_data_handling">Telecommunications and data handling</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Telecommunications and data handling">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:New_Horizons_-_REX.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="586" data-file-width="882" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/New_Horizons_-_REX.jpeg/220px-New_Horizons_-_REX.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/New_Horizons_-_REX.jpeg/330px-New_Horizons_-_REX.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/New_Horizons_-_REX.jpeg/440px-New_Horizons_-_REX.jpeg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:New_Horizons_-_REX.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>New Horizons</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'</span> <a href="/wiki/Antenna_(radio)" title="Antenna (radio)">antennas</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Communication with the spacecraft is via <a href="/wiki/X_band" title="X band">X band</a>. The craft had a communication rate of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7004380000000000000&#9824;</span>38&#160;kbit/s</span> at Jupiter; at Pluto\'s distance, a rate of approximately <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7003100000000000000&#9824;</span>1&#160;<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kilobit_per_second" title="Kilobit per second">kbit/s</a></span> per transmitter was expected. Besides the low data rate, Pluto\'s distance also causes a <a href="/wiki/Latency_(engineering)" title="Latency (engineering)">latency</a> of about 4.5&#160;hours (one-way). The 70&#160;m (230&#160;ft) <a href="/wiki/NASA_Deep_Space_Network" title="NASA Deep Space Network">NASA Deep Space Network</a> (DSN) dishes are used to relay commands once it is beyond Jupiter.  The spacecraft uses <a href="/wiki/Dual_modular_redundancy" title="Dual modular redundancy">dual modular redundancy</a> transmitters and receivers, and either right- or left-hand <a href="/wiki/Circular_polarization" title="Circular polarization">circular polarization</a>.  The downlink signal is amplified by dual redundant 12-watt <a href="/wiki/Traveling-wave_tube" title="Traveling-wave tube">traveling-wave tube</a> amplifiers (TWTAs) mounted on the body under the dish. The receivers are new, low-power designs. The system can be controlled to power both TWTAs at the same time, and transmit a dual-polarized downlink signal to the DSN that nearly doubles the downlink rate. DSN tests early in the mission with this dual polarization combining technique were successful, and the capability is now considered operational (when the spacecraft power budget permits both TWTAs to be powered).\n</p><p>In addition to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High-gain_antenna" title="High-gain antenna">high-gain antenna</a>, there are two backup low-gain antennas and a medium-gain dish. The high-gain dish has a <a href="/wiki/Cassegrain_reflector" title="Cassegrain reflector">Cassegrain reflector</a> layout, composite construction, of 2.1-meter (7&#160;ft) diameter providing over <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7001420000000000000&#9824;</span>42&#160;<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/DBi" title="DBi">dBi</a></span> of gain and a half-power beam width of about a degree. The prime-focus medium-gain antenna, with a 0.3-meter (1&#160;ft) aperture and 10&#176; half-power beam width, is mounted to the back of the high-gain antenna\'s secondary reflector. The forward low-gain antenna is stacked atop the feed of the medium-gain antenna. The aft low-gain antenna is mounted within the launch adapter at the rear of the spacecraft. This antenna was used only for early mission phases near Earth, just after launch and for emergencies if the spacecraft had lost attitude control.\n</p><p><i>New Horizons</i> recorded scientific instrument data to its solid-state memory buffer at each encounter, then transmitted the data to Earth. Data storage is done on two low-power <a href="/wiki/Flash_memory" title="Flash memory">solid-state recorders</a> (one primary, one backup) holding up to <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7000800000000000000&#9824;</span>8&#160;<a href="/wiki/Gigabyte" title="Gigabyte">gigabyte</a></span>s each. Because of the extreme distance from Pluto and the Kuiper belt, only one buffer load at those encounters can be saved. This is because <i>New Horizons</i> would require approximately 16 months after leaving the vicinity of Pluto to transmit the buffer load back to Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JHU_APL_News_Center_20150414_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-JHU_APL_News_Center_20150414-64">[64]</a></sup> At Pluto\'s distance, radio signals from the space probe back to Earth took four hours and 25 minutes to traverse 4.7&#160;billion km of space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Part of the reason for the delay between the gathering of and transmission of data is that all of the <i>New Horizons</i> instrumentation is body-mounted. In order for the cameras to record data, the entire probe must turn, and the one-degree-wide beam of the high-gain antenna was not pointing toward Earth.  Previous spacecraft, such as the <i>Voyager</i> program probes, had a rotatable instrumentation platform (a "scan platform") that could take measurements from virtually any angle without losing radio contact with Earth.  <i>New Horizons</i> was mechanically simplified to save weight, shorten the schedule, and improve reliability during its 15-year lifetime.\n</p><p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Voyager_2" title="Voyager 2">Voyager 2</a></i> scan platform jammed at Saturn, and the demands of long time exposures at outer planets led to a change of plans such that the entire probe was rotated to make photos at Uranus and Neptune, similar to how <i>New Horizons</i> rotated.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Science_payload">Science payload</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Science payload">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p><i>New Horizons</i> carries seven instruments: three optical instruments, two plasma instruments, a dust sensor and a radio science receiver/radiometer. The instruments are to be used to investigate the global geology, surface composition, surface temperature, atmospheric pressure, atmospheric temperature and escape rate of Pluto and its moons. The rated power is <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7001210000000000000&#9824;</span>21&#160;watts</span>, though not all instruments operate simultaneously.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> In addition, <i>New Horizons</i> has an Ultrastable Oscillator subsystem, which may be used to study and test the <a href="/wiki/Pioneer_anomaly" title="Pioneer anomaly">Pioneer anomaly</a> towards the end of the spacecraft\'s life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Long-Range_Reconnaissance_Imager_.28LORRI.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Long-Range_Reconnaissance_Imager_(LORRI)">Long-Range Reconnaissance Imager (LORRI)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Long-Range Reconnaissance Imager (LORRI)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:New_Horizons_LORRI.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1575" data-file-width="2100" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/New_Horizons_LORRI.jpg/220px-New_Horizons_LORRI.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/New_Horizons_LORRI.jpg/330px-New_Horizons_LORRI.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/New_Horizons_LORRI.jpg/440px-New_Horizons_LORRI.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:New_Horizons_LORRI.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>LORRI&#8212;long-range camera</div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Long_Range_Reconnaissance_Imager" title="Long Range Reconnaissance Imager">Long Range Reconnaissance Imager</a></div>\n<p>The Long-Range Reconnaissance Imager (LORRI) is a long-focal-length imager designed for high resolution and responsivity at visible wavelengths. The instrument is equipped with a 1024&#215;1024 pixel by 12-bits-per-pixel monochromatic <a href="/wiki/Charge-coupled_device" title="Charge-coupled device">CCD</a> imager giving a resolution of 5<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microradian" title="Microradian">&#956;rad</a> (~1<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arcsecond" title="Arcsecond">arcsec</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jhuapl-lorri_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-jhuapl-lorri-68">[68]</a></sup> The CCD is chilled far below freezing by a passive radiator on the antisolar face of the spacecraft. This temperature differential requires insulation, and isolation from the rest of the structure. The 208.3&#160;mm (8.20&#160;in) aperture <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ritchey-Chr%C3%A9tien_telescope" title="Ritchey-Chr&#233;tien telescope">Ritchey&#8211;Chretien</a> mirrors and metering structure are made of <a href="/wiki/Silicon_carbide" title="Silicon carbide">silicon carbide</a>, to boost stiffness, reduce weight, and prevent warping at low temperatures. The optical elements sit in a composite light shield, and mount with titanium and fiberglass for thermal isolation. Overall mass is 8.6&#160;kg (19&#160;lb), with the optical tube assembly (OTA) weighing about 5.6&#160;kg (12&#160;lb),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> for one of the largest silicon-carbide telescopes flown at the time (now surpassed by <a href="/wiki/Herschel_Space_Observatory" title="Herschel Space Observatory">Herschel</a>). For viewing on public web sites the 12-bit per pixel LORRI images are converted to 8-bit per pixel <a href="/wiki/JPEG" title="JPEG">JPEG</a> images.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jhuapl-lorri_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-jhuapl-lorri-68">[68]</a></sup> These public images do not contain the full <a href="/wiki/Dynamic_range" title="Dynamic range">dynamic range</a> of brightness information available from the raw LORRI images files.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jhuapl-lorri_68-2"><a href="#cite_note-jhuapl-lorri-68">[68]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span style="font-size:85%;"><i>Principal investigator: Andy Cheng, <a href="/wiki/Applied_Physics_Laboratory" title="Applied Physics Laboratory">Applied Physics Laboratory</a></i></span>, <span style="font-size:85%;"><i>Data: LORRI image search at jhuapl.edu</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup></span></dd></dl>\n<h3><span id="Solar_Wind_Around_Pluto_.28SWAP.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Solar_Wind_Around_Pluto_(SWAP)">Solar Wind Around Pluto (SWAP)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Solar Wind Around Pluto (SWAP)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:New_Horizons_SWAP.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1584" data-file-width="1824" decoding="async" height="191" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/New_Horizons_SWAP.jpg/220px-New_Horizons_SWAP.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/New_Horizons_SWAP.jpg/330px-New_Horizons_SWAP.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/New_Horizons_SWAP.jpg/440px-New_Horizons_SWAP.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:New_Horizons_SWAP.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/SWAP_(New_Horizons)" title="SWAP (New Horizons)">SWAP</a> &#8211; Solar Wind Around Pluto</div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/SWAP_(New_Horizons)" title="SWAP (New Horizons)">SWAP (New Horizons)</a></div>\n<p>Solar Wind Around Pluto (SWAP) is a toroidal <a href="/wiki/Electrostatic_analyzer" title="Electrostatic analyzer">electrostatic analyzer</a> and retarding potential analyzer (RPA), that makes up one of the two instruments comprising <i>New Horizons</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'</span> <a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" title="Plasma (physics)">Plasma</a> and high-energy particle spectrometer suite (PAM), the other being PEPSSI. SWAP measures particles of up to 6.5<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>keV and, because of the tenuous solar wind at Pluto\'s distance, the instrument is designed with the largest <a href="/wiki/Aperture" title="Aperture">aperture</a> of any such instrument ever flown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ProbeHomepage_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-ProbeHomepage-71">[71]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span style="font-size:85%;"><i>Principal investigator: David McComas, <a href="/wiki/Southwest_Research_Institute" title="Southwest Research Institute">Southwest Research Institute</a></i></span></dd></dl>\n<h3><span id="Pluto_Energetic_Particle_Spectrometer_Science_Investigation_.28PEPSSI.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Pluto_Energetic_Particle_Spectrometer_Science_Investigation_(PEPSSI)">Pluto Energetic Particle Spectrometer Science Investigation (PEPSSI)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Pluto Energetic Particle Spectrometer Science Investigation (PEPSSI)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pluto_Energetic_Particle_Spectrometer_Science_Investigation" title="Pluto Energetic Particle Spectrometer Science Investigation">Pluto Energetic Particle Spectrometer Science Investigation</a></div>\n<p>Pluto Energetic Particle Spectrometer Science Investigation (PEPSSI) is a <a href="/wiki/Time-of-flight_mass_spectrometry" title="Time-of-flight mass spectrometry">time of flight</a> <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electron</a> sensor that makes up one of the two instruments comprising <i>New Horizons</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'</span> plasma and high-energy particle spectrometer suite (PAM), the other being SWAP. Unlike SWAP, which measures particles of up to 6.5<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>keV, PEPSSI goes up to 1<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>MeV.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ProbeHomepage_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-ProbeHomepage-71">[71]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span style="font-size:85%;"><i>Principal investigator: Ralph McNutt Jr., Applied Physics Laboratory</i></span></dd></dl>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alice">Alice</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Alice">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Alice_(spacecraft_instrument)" title="Alice (spacecraft instrument)">Alice (spacecraft instrument)</a></div>\n<p><i>Alice</i> is an <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a> imaging <a href="/wiki/Spectrometer" title="Spectrometer">spectrometer</a> that is one of two photographic instruments comprising <i>New Horizons</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'</span> Pluto Exploration Remote Sensing Investigation (PERSI); the other being the <i>Ralph</i> telescope. It resolves 1,024<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>wavelength bands in the far and extreme ultraviolet (from 50&#8211;<span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6993180000000000000&#9824;</span>180&#160;<a href="/wiki/Nanometre" title="Nanometre">nm</a></span>), over 32<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>view fields. Its goal is to determine the composition of Pluto\'s atmosphere. This Alice instrument is derived from another Alice aboard <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">ESA</a>\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rosetta_space_probe#Nucleus" title="Rosetta space probe">Rosetta</a> spacecraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ProbeHomepage_71-2"><a href="#cite_note-ProbeHomepage-71">[71]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span style="font-size:85%;"><i>Principal investigator: Alan Stern, Southwest Research Institute</i></span></dd></dl>\n<p>In August 2018, NASA confirmed, based on results by <i>Alice</i> on the <i>New Horizons</i> spacecraft, a "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrogen_wall" title="Hydrogen wall">hydrogen wall</a>" at the outer edges of the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> that was first detected in 1992 by the two <a href="/wiki/Voyager_program" title="Voyager program">Voyager spacecraft</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GRL-20180807_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-GRL-20180807-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LS-20180809_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-LS-20180809-27">[27]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ralph_telescope">Ralph telescope</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Ralph telescope">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:New_Horizons_-_Ralph.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="587" data-file-width="880" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/New_Horizons_-_Ralph.png/220px-New_Horizons_-_Ralph.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/New_Horizons_-_Ralph.png/330px-New_Horizons_-_Ralph.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/New_Horizons_-_Ralph.png/440px-New_Horizons_-_Ralph.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:New_Horizons_-_Ralph.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Ralph</i>&#8212;telescope and color camera</div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ralph_(New_Horizons)" title="Ralph (New Horizons)">Ralph (New Horizons)</a></div>\n<p>The Ralph telescope,  75 mm<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> in aperture, is one of two photographic instruments that make up <i>New Horizons</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'</span> Pluto Exploration Remote Sensing Investigation (PERSI), with the other being the Alice instrument. Ralph has two separate channels: MVIC (Multispectral Visible Imaging Camera), a visible-light <a href="/wiki/Charge-coupled_device" title="Charge-coupled device">CCD</a> imager with broadband and color channels; and LEISA (Linear Etalon Imaging Spectral Array), a near-<a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a> imaging spectrometer. LEISA is derived from a similar instrument on the <a href="/wiki/Earth_Observing-1" title="Earth Observing-1">Earth Observing-1</a> spacecraft. Ralph was named after Alice\'s husband on <i><a href="/wiki/The_Honeymooners" title="The Honeymooners">The Honeymooners</a></i>, and was designed after Alice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sn20150711_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-sn20150711-73">[73]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On June 23, 2017, NASA announced that it has renamed the LEISA instrument to the "Lisa Hardaway Infrared Mapping Spectrometer" in honor of <a href="/wiki/Lisa_Hardaway" title="Lisa Hardaway">Lisa Hardaway</a>, the Ralph program manager at <a href="/wiki/Ball_Aerospace_%26_Technologies" title="Ball Aerospace &amp; Technologies">Ball Aerospace</a>, who died in January 2017 at age 50.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa20170623_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa20170623-74">[74]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span style="font-size:85%;"><i>Principal investigator: Alan Stern, Southwest Research Institute</i></span></dd></dl>\n<h3><span id="Venetia_Burney_Student_Dust_Counter_.28VBSDC.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Venetia_Burney_Student_Dust_Counter_(VBSDC)">Venetia Burney Student Dust Counter (VBSDC)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Venetia Burney Student Dust Counter (VBSDC)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:New_Horizons_sdc.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1704" data-file-width="2272" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/New_Horizons_sdc.jpeg/220px-New_Horizons_sdc.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/New_Horizons_sdc.jpeg/330px-New_Horizons_sdc.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/New_Horizons_sdc.jpeg/440px-New_Horizons_sdc.jpeg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:New_Horizons_sdc.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>VBSDC&#8212;Venetia Burney Student Dust Counter</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Venetia_Burney_Student_Dust_Counter" title="Venetia Burney Student Dust Counter">Venetia Burney Student Dust Counter</a> (VBSDC), built by students at the University of Colorado Boulder, is operating periodically to make <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_dust" title="Cosmic dust">dust</a> measurements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> It consists of a detector panel, about 460&#160;mm &#215;&#160;300&#160;mm (18&#160;in &#215;&#160;12&#160;in), mounted on the anti-solar face of the spacecraft (the ram direction), and an electronics box within the spacecraft. The detector contains fourteen <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polyvinylidene_difluoride" title="Polyvinylidene difluoride">polyvinylidene difluoride</a> (PVDF) panels, twelve science and two reference, which generate voltage when impacted. Effective collecting area is 0.125&#160;m<sup>2</sup> (1.35&#160;sq&#160;ft). No dust counter has operated past the orbit of <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a>; models of dust in the outer Solar System, especially the Kuiper belt, are speculative. The VBSDC is always turned on measuring the masses of the interplanetary and interstellar dust particles (in the range of nano- and picograms) as they collide with the PVDF panels mounted on the <i>New Horizons</i> spacecraft. The measured data is expected to greatly contribute to the understanding of the dust spectra of the Solar System. The dust spectra can then be compared with those from observations of other stars, giving new clues as to where Earth-like planets can be found in the universe. The dust counter is named for <a href="/wiki/Venetia_Burney" title="Venetia Burney">Venetia Burney</a>, who first suggested the name "Pluto" at the age of 11. A thirteen-minute short film about the VBSDC garnered an Emmy Award for student achievement in 2006.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span style="font-size:85%;"><i>Principal investigator: Mihaly Horanyi, <a href="/wiki/University_of_Colorado_Boulder" title="University of Colorado Boulder">University of Colorado Boulder</a></i></span></dd></dl>\n<h3><span id="Radio_Science_Experiment_.28REX.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Radio_Science_Experiment_(REX)">Radio Science Experiment (REX)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Radio Science Experiment (REX)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/REX_(New_Horizons)" title="REX (New Horizons)">REX (New Horizons)</a></div>\n<p>The Radio Science Experiment (REX) used an ultrastable <a href="/wiki/Crystal_oscillator" title="Crystal oscillator">crystal oscillator</a> (essentially a calibrated crystal in a miniature <a href="/wiki/Crystal_oven" title="Crystal oven">oven</a>) and some additional electronics to conduct radio science investigations using the communications channels. These are small enough to fit on a single card. Because there are two redundant communications subsystems, there are two, identical REX circuit boards.\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span style="font-size:85%;"><i>Principal investigators: Len Tyler and Ivan Linscott, <a href="/wiki/Stanford_University" title="Stanford University">Stanford University</a></i></span></dd></dl>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Journey_to_Pluto">Journey to Pluto</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Journey to Pluto">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Launch">Launch</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Launch">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;max-width:292px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:154px;max-width:154px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:202px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NewHorizons_Rocket_Bly.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="1944" decoding="async" height="203" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/NewHorizons_Rocket_Bly.jpg/152px-NewHorizons_Rocket_Bly.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/NewHorizons_Rocket_Bly.jpg/228px-NewHorizons_Rocket_Bly.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/NewHorizons_Rocket_Bly.jpg/304px-NewHorizons_Rocket_Bly.jpg 2x" width="152"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:134px;max-width:134px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:202px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Atlas_V_551_roars_into_blue_sky.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="3008" data-file-width="1960" decoding="async" height="203" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Atlas_V_551_roars_into_blue_sky.jpg/132px-Atlas_V_551_roars_into_blue_sky.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Atlas_V_551_roars_into_blue_sky.jpg/198px-Atlas_V_551_roars_into_blue_sky.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Atlas_V_551_roars_into_blue_sky.jpg/264px-Atlas_V_551_roars_into_blue_sky.jpg 2x" width="132"/></a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">Launch of <i>New Horizons</i>. The <a href="/wiki/Atlas_V" title="Atlas V">Atlas V</a> rocket on the launchpad (left) and lift off from Cape Canaveral.</div></div></div>\n<p>On September 24, 2005, the spacecraft arrived at the Kennedy Space Center on board a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/C-17_Globemaster_III" title="C-17 Globemaster III">C-17 Globemaster III</a> for launch preparations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SpaceDaily_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-SpaceDaily-78">[78]</a></sup> The launch of <i>New Horizons</i> was originally scheduled for January 11, 2006, but was initially delayed until January 17, 2006, to allow for <a href="/wiki/Borescope" title="Borescope">borescope</a> inspections of the <a href="/wiki/Atlas_V" title="Atlas V">Atlas V</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Kerosene" title="Kerosene">kerosene</a> tank. Further delays related to low cloud ceiling conditions <a href="/wiki/Downrange" title="Downrange">downrange</a>, and high winds and technical difficulties&#8212;unrelated to the rocket itself&#8212;prevented launch for a further two days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes.com_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes.com-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Space.com_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Space.com-80">[80]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The probe finally lifted off from <a href="/wiki/Cape_Canaveral_Air_Force_Station_Space_Launch_Complex_41" title="Cape Canaveral Air Force Station Space Launch Complex 41">Pad 41</a> at <a href="/wiki/Cape_Canaveral_Air_Force_Station" title="Cape Canaveral Air Force Station">Cape Canaveral Air Force Station</a>, <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a>, directly south of <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">Space Shuttle</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Launch_Complex_39" title="Launch Complex 39">Launch Complex 39</a>, at 19:00&#160;UTC on January 19, 2006.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Amir_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Amir-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harwood_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harwood-82">[82]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Centaur_(rocket_stage)" title="Centaur (rocket stage)">Centaur</a> second stage ignited at 19:04:43&#160;UTC and burned for 5 minutes 25 seconds. It reignited at 19:32&#160;UTC and burned for 9 minutes 47 seconds. The <a href="/wiki/Alliant_Techsystems" title="Alliant Techsystems">ATK</a> <a href="/wiki/Star_48" title="Star 48">Star 48</a>B third stage ignited at 19:42:37&#160;UTC and burned for 1 minute 28 seconds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ils200601_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-ils200601-83">[83]</a></sup> Combined, these burns successfully sent the probe on a solar-escape trajectory at 16.26 kilometers per second (58,536&#160;km/h; 36,373&#160;mph).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sciam20130225_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-sciam20130225-11">[11]</a></sup> <i>New Horizons</i> took only nine hours to pass the Moon\'s orbit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-airspace20150710_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-airspace20150710-84">[84]</a></sup> Although there were backup launch opportunities in February 2006 and February 2007, only the first twenty-three days of the 2006 window permitted the Jupiter flyby. Any launch outside that period would have forced the spacecraft to fly a slower trajectory directly to Pluto, delaying its encounter by five to six years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa20150702_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa20150702-85">[85]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The probe was launched by a <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_Martin" title="Lockheed Martin">Lockheed Martin</a> Atlas V 551 rocket, with a third stage added to increase the heliocentric (escape) speed. This was the first launch of the Atlas V 551 configuration, which uses five <a href="/wiki/Solid_rocket_booster" title="Solid rocket booster">solid rocket boosters</a>, and the first Atlas V with a third stage. Previous flights had used zero, two, or three solid boosters, but never five. The vehicle, AV-010, weighed 573,160 kilograms (1,263,600&#160;lb) at lift-off,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ils200601_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-ils200601-83">[83]</a></sup> and had earlier been slightly damaged when <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Wilma" title="Hurricane Wilma">Hurricane Wilma</a> swept across Florida on October 24, 2005. One of the solid rocket boosters was hit by a door.
