The booster was replaced with an identical unit, rather than inspecting and requalifying the original.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The launch was dedicated to the memory of launch conductor <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Sarokon" title="Daniel Sarokon">Daniel Sarokon</a>, who was described by space program officials as one of the most influential people in the history of space travel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inner_Solar_System">Inner Solar System</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Inner Solar System">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Trajectory_corrections">Trajectory corrections</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Trajectory corrections">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>On January 28 and 30, 2006, mission controllers guided the probe through its first <a href="/wiki/Trajectory" title="Trajectory">trajectory</a>-correction maneuver (TCM), which was divided into two parts (TCM-1A and TCM-1B). The total velocity change of these two corrections was about 18 meters per second (65&#160;km/h; 40&#160;mph). TCM-1 was accurate enough to permit the cancellation of TCM-2, the second of three originally scheduled corrections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> On March 9, 2006, controllers performed TCM-3, the last of three scheduled course corrections. The engines burned for 76&#160;seconds, adjusting the spacecraft\'s velocity by about 1.16&#160;m/s (4.2&#160;km/h; 2.6&#160;mph).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> Further trajectory maneuvers were not needed until September 25, 2007 (seven months after the Jupiter flyby), when the engines were fired for 15&#160;minutes and 37&#160;seconds, changing the spacecraft\'s velocity by 2.37&#160;m/s (8.5&#160;km/h; 5.3&#160;mph),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> followed by another TCM, almost three years later on June 30, 2010, that lasted 35.6&#160;seconds, when <i>New Horizons</i> had already reached the halfway point (in time traveled) to Pluto.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="In-flight_tests_and_crossing_of_Mars_orbit">In-flight tests and crossing of Mars orbit</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: In-flight tests and crossing of Mars orbit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>During the week of February 20, 2006, controllers conducted initial in-flight tests of three onboard science instruments, the Alice ultraviolet imaging spectrometer, the PEPSSI plasma-sensor, and the LORRI long-range visible-spectrum camera. No scientific measurements or images were taken, but instrument electronics, and in the case of Alice, some electromechanical systems were shown to be functioning correctly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On April 7, 2006, the spacecraft passed the orbit of Mars, moving at roughly 21&#160;km/s (76,000&#160;km/h; 47,000&#160;mph) away from the Sun at a solar distance of 243 million kilometers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Malik_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Malik-93">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Asteroid_132524_APL">Asteroid 132524 APL</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Asteroid 132524 APL">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;max-width:292px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:144px;max-width:144px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:142px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Asteroid_2002_JF56.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="300" decoding="async" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Asteroid_2002_JF56.jpg/142px-Asteroid_2002_JF56.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Asteroid_2002_JF56.jpg/213px-Asteroid_2002_JF56.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Asteroid_2002_JF56.jpg/284px-Asteroid_2002_JF56.jpg 2x" width="142"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Asteroid <a href="/wiki/132524_APL" title="132524 APL">132524 APL</a> viewed by <i>New Horizons</i> in June 2006</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:144px;max-width:144px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:142px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:First_Pluto_sighting_from_New_Horizons.gif"><img alt="" data-file-height="896" data-file-width="896" decoding="async" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/First_Pluto_sighting_from_New_Horizons.gif/142px-First_Pluto_sighting_from_New_Horizons.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/First_Pluto_sighting_from_New_Horizons.gif/213px-First_Pluto_sighting_from_New_Horizons.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/First_Pluto_sighting_from_New_Horizons.gif/284px-First_Pluto_sighting_from_New_Horizons.gif 2x" width="142"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">First images of Pluto in September 2006</div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div>\n<p>Because of the need to conserve fuel for possible encounters with Kuiper belt objects subsequent to the Pluto flyby, intentional encounters with objects in the <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_belt" title="Asteroid belt">asteroid belt</a> were not planned. After launch, the <i>New Horizons</i> team scanned the spacecraft\'s trajectory to determine if any asteroids would, by chance, be close enough for observation. In May 2006 it was discovered that <i>New Horizons</i> would pass close to the tiny asteroid <a href="/wiki/132524_APL" title="132524 APL">132524 APL</a> on June 13, 2006. Closest approach occurred at 4:05&#160;UTC at a distance of 101,867&#160;km (63,297&#160;mi). The asteroid was imaged by Ralph (use of LORRI was not possible because of proximity to the Sun), which gave the team a chance to test Ralph<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> capabilities, and make observations of the asteroid\'s composition as well as light and phase curves. The asteroid was estimated to be 2.5&#160;km (1.6&#160;mi) in diameter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JHAPL61506_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-JHAPL61506-98">[98]</a></sup> The spacecraft successfully tracked the rapidly moving asteroid over June 10&#8211;12, 2006.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="First_Pluto_sighting">First Pluto sighting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: First Pluto sighting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The first images of Pluto from <i>New Horizons</i> were acquired September 21&#8211;24, 2006, during a test of LORRI. They were released on November 28, 2006.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LORRI_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-LORRI-99">[99]</a></sup> The images, taken from a distance of approximately 4.2&#160;billion&#160;km (2.6&#160;billion&#160;mi; 28&#160;AU), confirmed the spacecraft\'s ability to track distant targets, critical for maneuvering toward Pluto and other Kuiper belt objects.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Jupiter_encounter">Jupiter encounter</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Jupiter encounter">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Exploration_of_Jupiter" title="Exploration of Jupiter">Exploration of Jupiter</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:132px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NH_Jupiter_IR_(contrast_enhanced).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="481" data-file-width="253" decoding="async" height="247" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/NH_Jupiter_IR_%28contrast_enhanced%29.jpg/130px-NH_Jupiter_IR_%28contrast_enhanced%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/NH_Jupiter_IR_%28contrast_enhanced%29.jpg/195px-NH_Jupiter_IR_%28contrast_enhanced%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/db/NH_Jupiter_IR_%28contrast_enhanced%29.jpg 2x" width="130"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NH_Jupiter_IR_(contrast_enhanced).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Infrared image of Jupiter by <i>New Horizons</i></div></div></div>\n<p><i>New Horizons</i> used LORRI to take its first photographs of Jupiter on September 4, 2006, from a distance of 291&#160;million kilometers (181&#160;million miles).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> More detailed exploration of the system began in January 2007 with an infrared image of the moon <a href="/wiki/Callisto_(moon)" title="Callisto (moon)">Callisto</a>, as well as several black-and-white images of Jupiter itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jupiter-encounter_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-jupiter-encounter-101">[101]</a></sup> <i>New Horizons</i> received a gravity assist from Jupiter, with its closest approach at 05:43:40&#160;UTC on February 28, 2007, when it was 2.3&#160;million kilometers (1.4&#160;million miles) from Jupiter. The flyby increased <i>New Horizons</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'</span> speed by 4&#160;km/s (14,000&#160;km/h; 9,000&#160;mph), accelerating the probe to a velocity of 23&#160;km/s (83,000&#160;km/h; 51,000&#160;mph) relative to the Sun and shortening its voyage to Pluto by three years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-boost_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-boost-102">[102]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The flyby was the center of a four-month intensive observation campaign lasting from January to June. Being an ever-changing scientific target, Jupiter has been observed intermittently since the end of the <i><a href="/wiki/Galileo_(spacecraft)" title="Galileo (spacecraft)">Galileo</a></i> mission in September 2003. Knowledge about Jupiter benefited from the fact that <i>New Horizons</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'</span> instruments were built using the latest technology, especially in the area of cameras, representing a significant improvement over <i>Galileo</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> cameras, which were modified versions of <i><a href="/wiki/Voyager_1" title="Voyager 1">Voyager</a></i> cameras, which, in turn, were modified <i><a href="/wiki/Mariner_program" title="Mariner program">Mariner</a></i> cameras. The Jupiter encounter also served as a shakedown and dress rehearsal for the Pluto encounter. Because Jupiter is much closer to Earth than Pluto, the communications link can transmit multiple loadings of the memory buffer; thus the mission returned more data from the Jovian system than it was expected to transmit from Pluto.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup>\n</p><p>One of the main goals during the Jupiter encounter was observing its <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Jupiter" title="Atmosphere of Jupiter">atmospheric conditions</a> and analyzing the structure and composition of its clouds. Heat-induced lightning strikes in the polar regions and "waves" that indicate violent storm activity were observed and measured. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oval_BA" title="Oval BA">Little Red Spot</a>, spanning up to 70% of Earth\'s diameter, was imaged from up close for the first time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-boost_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-boost-102">[102]</a></sup> Recording from different angles and illumination conditions, <i>New Horizons</i> took detailed images of Jupiter\'s faint <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Jupiter" title="Rings of Jupiter">ring system</a>, discovering debris left over from recent collisions within the rings or from other unexplained phenomena. The search for undiscovered moons within the rings showed no results. Travelling through Jupiter\'s <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere_of_Jupiter" title="Magnetosphere of Jupiter">magnetosphere</a>, <i>New Horizons</i> collected valuable particle readings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-boost_102-2"><a href="#cite_note-boost-102">[102]</a></sup> "Bubbles" of plasma that are thought to be formed from material ejected by the moon Io, were noticed in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetotail" title="Magnetotail">magnetotail</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-surf_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-surf-104">[104]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Jovian_moons">Jovian moons</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Jovian moons">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The four largest moons of Jupiter were in poor positions for observation; the necessary path of the gravity-assist maneuver meant that <i>New Horizons</i> passed millions of kilometers from any of the <a href="/wiki/Galilean_moons" title="Galilean moons">Galilean moons</a>. Still, its instruments were intended for small, dim targets, so they were scientifically useful on large, distant moons. Emphasis was put on Jupiter\'s innermost Galilean moon, <a href="/wiki/Io_(moon)" title="Io (moon)">Io</a>, whose active volcanoes shoot out tons of material into Jupiter\'s magnetosphere, and further. Out of eleven observed eruptions, three were seen for the first time. That of <a href="/wiki/Tvashtar_Paterae" title="Tvashtar Paterae">Tvashtar</a> reached an altitude of up to 330&#160;km (210&#160;mi). The event gave scientists an unprecedented look into the structure and motion of the rising plume and its subsequent fall back to the surface. Infrared signatures of a further 36 volcanoes were noticed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-boost_102-3"><a href="#cite_note-boost-102">[102]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Callisto_(moon)" title="Callisto (moon)">Callisto</a>\'s surface was analyzed with LEISA, revealing how lighting and viewing conditions affect infrared spectrum readings of its surface water ice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> Minor moons such as <a href="/wiki/Amalthea_(moon)" title="Amalthea (moon)">Amalthea</a> had their orbit solutions refined. The cameras determined their positions, acting as "reverse optical navigation".\n</p>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tnone center"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:792px;max-width:792px"><div style="clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;background-color:transparent">Jovian moons imaged by <i>New Horizons</i></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:196px;max-width:196px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:194px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tvashtar_volcano_on_Io_from_New_Horizons.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="420" data-file-width="420" decoding="async" height="194" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Tvashtar_volcano_on_Io_from_New_Horizons.jpg/194px-Tvashtar_volcano_on_Io_from_New_Horizons.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Tvashtar_volcano_on_Io_from_New_Horizons.jpg/291px-Tvashtar_volcano_on_Io_from_New_Horizons.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Tvashtar_volcano_on_Io_from_New_Horizons.jpg/388px-Tvashtar_volcano_on_Io_from_New_Horizons.jpg 2x" width="194"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Io_(moon)" title="Io (moon)">Io</a> imaged on February 28, 2007. The feature near the north pole of the moon is a 290&#160;km (180&#160;mi) high plume from the volcano <a href="/wiki/Tvashtar_Paterae" title="Tvashtar Paterae">Tvashtar</a>.</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:196px;max-width:196px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:194px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA09246_Europa.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="256" data-file-width="256" decoding="async" height="194" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/PIA09246_Europa.jpg/194px-PIA09246_Europa.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/ec/PIA09246_Europa.jpg 1.5x" width="194"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">Europa</a> imaged on February 27, 2007, from a distance of 3.1&#160;million&#160;km (1.9&#160;million&#160;mi). Image scale is 15&#160;km per pixel (9.3&#160;mi/px).</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:196px;max-width:196px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:194px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA09245_Ganymede.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="380" data-file-width="380" decoding="async" height="194" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/PIA09245_Ganymede.jpg/194px-PIA09245_Ganymede.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/PIA09245_Ganymede.jpg/291px-PIA09245_Ganymede.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/bc/PIA09245_Ganymede.jpg 2x" width="194"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Ganymede_(moon)" title="Ganymede (moon)">Ganymede</a> imaged on February 27, 2007, from a distance of 3.5&#160;million&#160;km (2.2&#160;million&#160;mi). Image scale is 17&#160;km per pixel (11&#160;mi/px).</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:196px;max-width:196px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:194px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA09258_Callisto.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="266" data-file-width="266" decoding="async" height="194" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/PIA09258_Callisto.jpg/194px-PIA09258_Callisto.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/2e/PIA09258_Callisto.jpg 1.5x" width="194"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Callisto_(moon)" title="Callisto (moon)">Callisto</a> imaged on February 27, 2007, from a distance of 4.7&#160;million&#160;km (2.9&#160;million&#160;mi).</div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Commons-logo.svg"><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="1376" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="16" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4a/Commons-logo.svg/24px-Commons-logo.svg.png 2x" width="12"/></a> Media related to <a class="extiw" href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/Category:Photos_of_Jupiter_system_by_New_Horizons" title="commons:Category:Photos of Jupiter system by New Horizons">Photos of Jupiter system by New Horizons</a> at Wikimedia Commons</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Outer_Solar_System">Outer Solar System</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Outer Solar System">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0a/Interstellar_probes_trajectory.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="375" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Interstellar_probes_trajectory.svg/250px-Interstellar_probes_trajectory.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Interstellar_probes_trajectory.svg/375px-Interstellar_probes_trajectory.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Interstellar_probes_trajectory.svg/500px-Interstellar_probes_trajectory.svg.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Interstellar_probes_trajectory.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ecliptic_coordinate_system#Spherical_coordinates" title="Ecliptic coordinate system">Heliocentric positions</a> of the five <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_probe" title="Interstellar probe">interstellar probes</a> (squares) and other bodies (circles) until 2020, with launch and flyby dates. Markers denote positions on <span class="nowrap">1 January</span> of each year, with every fifth year labelled.<br style="margin-bottom:1ex;"/><span class="nowrap">Plot 1</span> is viewed from the <a href="/wiki/Orbital_pole#Ecliptic_pole" title="Orbital pole">north ecliptic pole</a>, to scale; <span class="nowrap">plots 2 to 4</span> are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Third-angle_projection" title="Third-angle projection">third-angle projections</a> at 20% scale.<br style="margin-bottom:1ex;"/>In <a class="external text" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0a/Interstellar_probes_trajectory.svg"><span class="nowrap">the SVG file,</span></a> hover over a trajectory or orbit to highlight it and its associated launches and flybys.</div></div></div>\n<p>After passing Jupiter, <i>New Horizons</i> spent most of its journey towards Pluto in hibernation mode: redundant components as well as guidance and control systems were shut down to extend their life cycle, decrease operation costs and free the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deep_Space_Network" title="Deep Space Network">Deep Space Network</a> for other missions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hibernation1_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-hibernation1-106">[106]</a></sup> During hibernation mode, the onboard computer monitored the probe\'s systems and transmitted a signal back to Earth: a "green" code if everything was functioning as expected or a "red" code if mission control\'s assistance was needed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hibernation1_106-1"><a href="#cite_note-hibernation1-106">[106]</a></sup> The probe was activated for about two months a year so that the instruments could be calibrated and the systems checked. The first hibernation mode cycle started on June 28, 2007,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hibernation1_106-2"><a href="#cite_note-hibernation1-106">[106]</a></sup> the second cycle began on December 16, 2008,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> the third cycle on August 27, 2009,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> and the fourth cycle on August 29, 2014, after a 10-week test.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup>\n</p><p><i>New Horizons</i> crossed the orbit of <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a> on June 8, 2008,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-saturn_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-saturn-110">[110]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a> on March 18, 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uranus_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-uranus-111">[111]</a></sup> After astronomers announced the discovery of two new moons in the Pluto system, <a href="/wiki/Kerberos_(moon)" title="Kerberos (moon)">Kerberos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Styx_(moon)" title="Styx (moon)">Styx</a>, mission planners started contemplating the possibility of the probe running into unseen debris and dust left over from ancient collisions between the moons. A study based on 18 months of computer simulations, Earth-based telescope observations and occultations of the Pluto system revealed that the possibility of a catastrophic collision with debris or dust was less than 0.3% on the probe\'s scheduled course.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-debris1_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-debris1-112">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-debris2_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-debris2-113">[113]</a></sup> If the hazard increased, <i>New Horizons</i> could have used one of two possible contingency plans, the so-called SHBOTs (Safe Haven by Other Trajectories): the probe could have continued on its present trajectory with the antenna facing the incoming particles so the more vital systems would be protected, or, it could have positioned its antenna to make a course correction that would take it just 3000&#160;km from the surface of Pluto where it was expected that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atmospheric_drag" title="Atmospheric drag">atmospheric drag</a> would have cleaned the surrounding space of possible debris.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-debris2_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-debris2-113">[113]</a></sup>\n</p><p>While in hibernation mode in July 2012, <i>New Horizons</i> started gathering scientific data with SWAP, PEPSSI and VBSDC. Although it was originally planned to activate just the VBSDC, other instruments were powered on the initiative of principal investigator Alan Stern who decided they could use the opportunity to collect valuable heliospheric data. Before activating the other two instruments, ground tests were conducted to make sure that the expanded data gathering in this phase of the mission would not limit available energy, memory and fuel in the future and that all systems are functioning during the flyby.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-new_instruments_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-new_instruments-114">[114]</a></sup> The first set of data was transmitted in January 2013 during a three-week activation from hibernation. The command and data handling software was updated to address the problem of computer resets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-data_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-data-115">[115]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Possible_Neptune_trojan_targets"><span id="New_Horizons_KBO_Search"></span> Possible Neptune trojan targets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Possible Neptune trojan targets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Other possible targets were <a href="/wiki/Neptune_trojan" title="Neptune trojan">Neptune trojans</a>. The probe\'s trajectory to Pluto passed near Neptune\'s trailing <a href="/wiki/Lagrangian_point" title="Lagrangian point">Lagrange point</a> ("<a href="/wiki/Lagrangian_point#L4_and_L5" title="Lagrangian point">L<sub>5</sub></a>"), which may host hundreds of bodies in 1:1 <a href="/wiki/Orbital_resonance" title="Orbital resonance">resonance</a>. In late 2013, <i>New Horizons</i> passed within 1.2&#160;AU (180,000,000&#160;km; 110,000,000&#160;mi) of the high-inclination L5 Neptune trojan <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/2011_HM102" title="2011 HM102">2011 HM<span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;"><span style="font-size:80%;">102</span></span></a></span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-parker2012_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-parker2012-116">[116]</a></sup> which was identified shortly before by the <b>New Horizons KBO Search</b> survey team while searching for more distant objects for <i>New Horizons</i> to fly by after its 2015 Pluto encounter. At that range, <span class="nowrap">2011 HM<sub>102</sub></span> would have been bright enough to be detectable by <i>New Horizons</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'</span> LORRI instrument; however, the <i>New Horizons</i> team eventually decided that they would not target <span class="nowrap">2011 HM<sub>102</sub></span> for observations because the preparations for the Pluto approach took precedence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span id="Observations_of_Pluto_and_Charon_2013.E2.80.9314"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Observations_of_Pluto_and_Charon_2013&#8211;14">Observations of Pluto and Charon 2013&#8211;14</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Observations of Pluto and Charon 2013&#8211;14">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Images from July 1 to 3, 2013, by LORRI were the first by the probe to resolve Pluto and Charon as separate objects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> On July 14, 2014, mission controllers performed a sixth trajectory-correction maneuver (TCM) since its launch to enable the craft to reach Pluto.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> Between July 19&#8211;24, 2014, <i>New Horizons</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'</span> LORRI snapped 12 images of Charon revolving around Pluto, covering almost one full rotation at distances ranging from about 429 to 422&#160;million kilometers (267,000,000 to 262,000,000&#160;mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> In August 2014, astronomers made high-precision measurements of Pluto\'s location and orbit around the Sun using the Atacama Large Millimeter/submillimeter Array (<a href="/wiki/Atacama_Large_Millimeter_Array" title="Atacama Large Millimeter Array">ALMA</a>) to help NASA\'s <i>New Horizons</i> spacecraft accurately home in on Pluto.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> On December 6, 2014, mission controllers sent a signal for the craft to "wake up" from its final Pluto-approach hibernation and begin regular operations. The craft\'s response that it was "awake" arrived to Earth on December 7, 2014, at 02:30&#160;UTC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-awoketwitter_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-awoketwitter-122">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-awokeAST_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-awokeAST-123">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pluto_approach">Pluto approach</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Pluto approach">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;max-width:292px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:142px;max-width:142px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:139px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PlutoCharon-1stColorImage-NewHorizons-Ralph-20150409.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/PlutoCharon-1stColorImage-NewHorizons-Ralph-20150409.png/140px-PlutoCharon-1stColorImage-NewHorizons-Ralph-20150409.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/PlutoCharon-1stColorImage-NewHorizons-Ralph-20150409.png/210px-PlutoCharon-1stColorImage-NewHorizons-Ralph-20150409.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/PlutoCharon-1stColorImage-NewHorizons-Ralph-20150409.png/280px-PlutoCharon-1stColorImage-NewHorizons-Ralph-20150409.png 2x" width="140"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:146px;max-width:146px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:139px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pluto-System-June-29.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="386" data-file-width="399" decoding="async" height="139" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Pluto-System-June-29.png/144px-Pluto-System-June-29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Pluto-System-June-29.png/216px-Pluto-System-June-29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Pluto-System-June-29.png/288px-Pluto-System-June-29.png 2x" width="144"/></a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent"><a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a> and <a href="/wiki/Charon_(moon)" title="Charon (moon)">Charon</a> photographed on April 9, 2015, <i>(left)</i> by <a href="/wiki/Ralph_(New_Horizons)" title="Ralph (New Horizons)">Ralph</a> and on June 29, 2015, <i>(right)</i> by <a href="/wiki/Long_Range_Reconnaissance_Imager" title="Long Range Reconnaissance Imager">LORRI</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Distant-encounter operations at Pluto began on January 4, 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> At this date images of the targets with the onboard LORRI imager plus the Ralph telescope would only be a few <a href="/wiki/Pixel" title="Pixel">pixels</a> in width. Investigators began taking Pluto and background starfield images to assist mission navigators in the design of course-correcting engine maneuvers that would precisely modify the trajectory of <i>New Horizons</i> to aim the approach. On January 15, 2015, NASA gave a brief update of the timeline of the approach and departure phases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On February 12, 2015, NASA released new images of Pluto (taken from January 25 to 31) from the approaching probe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> <i>New Horizons</i> was more than 203&#160;million kilometers (126,000,000&#160;mi) away from Pluto when it began taking the photos, which showed Pluto and its largest moon, Charon. The exposure time was too short to see Pluto\'s smaller, much fainter, moons.\n</p><p>Investigators compiled a series of images of the moons Nix and Hydra taken from January 27 through February 8, 2015, beginning at a range of 201&#160;million kilometers (125,000,000&#160;mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> Pluto and Charon appear as a single overexposed object at the center. The right side image has been processed to remove the background starfield. The yet smaller two moons, Kerberos and Styx were seen on photos taken on April 25.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> Starting May 11 a hazard search was performed, by looking for unknown objects that could be a danger to the spacecraft, such as rings or more moons, which were possible to avoid by a course change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Also in regards to the approach phase during January 2015, on August 21, 2012, the team announced that they would spend mission time attempting long-range observations of the Kuiper belt object temporarily designated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/VNH0004" title="VNH0004">VNH0004</a> (now designated <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/2011_KW48" title="2011 KW48">2011 KW<span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;"><span style="font-size:80%;">48</span></span></a></span>), when the object was at a distance from <i>New Horizons</i> of 75 gigameters (0.50&#160;AU).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vnh_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-vnh-132">[132]</a></sup> The object would be too distant to resolve surface features or take spectroscopy, but it would be able to make observations that cannot be made from Earth, namely a <a href="/wiki/Phase_curve_(astronomy)" title="Phase curve (astronomy)">phase curve</a> and a search for small moons. A second object was planned to be observed in June 2015, and a third in September after the flyby; the team hoped to observe a dozen such objects through 2018.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vnh_132-1"><a href="#cite_note-vnh-132">[132]</a></sup> On April 15, 2015, Pluto was imaged showing a possible polar cap.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20150429_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20150429-133">[133]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Software_glitch">Software glitch</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Software glitch">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>On July 4, 2015, <i>New Horizons</i> experienced a software anomaly and went into safe mode, preventing the spacecraft from performing scientific observations until engineers could resolve the problem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> On July 5, NASA announced that the problem was determined to be a timing flaw in a command sequence used to prepare the spacecraft for its flyby, and the spacecraft would resume scheduled science operations on July 7. The science observations lost because of the anomaly were judged to have no impact on the mission\'s main objectives and minimal impact on other objectives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-new-horizons-2015-07-05_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-new-horizons-2015-07-05-136">[136]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The timing flaw consisted of performing two tasks simultaneously&#8212;compressing previously acquired data to release space for more data, and making a second copy of the approach command sequence&#8212;that together overloaded the spacecraft\'s primary computer. After the overload was detected, the spacecraft performed as designed: it switched from the primary computer to the backup computer, entered safe mode, and sent a distress call back to Earth. The distress call was received the afternoon of July 4, which alerted engineers that they needed to contact the spacecraft to get more information and resolve the issue. The resolution was that the problem happened as part of preparations for the approach, and was not expected to happen again because no similar tasks were planned for the remainder of the encounter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-new-horizons-2015-07-05_136-1"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-new-horizons-2015-07-05-136">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pluto_system_encounter">Pluto system encounter</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Pluto system encounter">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alan_Stern_and_New_Horizons_Team_Celebrate_Pluto_Flyby.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="992" data-file-width="1411" decoding="async" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Alan_Stern_and_New_Horizons_Team_Celebrate_Pluto_Flyby.jpg/220px-Alan_Stern_and_New_Horizons_Team_Celebrate_Pluto_Flyby.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Alan_Stern_and_New_Horizons_Team_Celebrate_Pluto_Flyby.jpg/330px-Alan_Stern_and_New_Horizons_Team_Celebrate_Pluto_Flyby.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Alan_Stern_and_New_Horizons_Team_Celebrate_Pluto_Flyby.jpg/440px-Alan_Stern_and_New_Horizons_Team_Celebrate_Pluto_Flyby.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alan_Stern_and_New_Horizons_Team_Celebrate_Pluto_Flyby.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Alan_Stern" title="Alan Stern">Alan Stern</a> and the <i>New Horizons</i> team celebrate after the spacecraft successfully completed its flyby of Pluto.</div></div></div>\n<p>The closest approach of the <i>New Horizons</i> spacecraft to Pluto occurred at 11:49&#160;UTC on July 14, 2015, at a range of 12,472&#160;km (7,750&#160;mi) from the surface<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> and 13,658&#160;km (8,487&#160;mi) from the center of Pluto. <a href="/wiki/Telemetry" title="Telemetry">Telemetry</a> data confirming a successful flyby and a healthy spacecraft were received on Earth from the vicinity of the Pluto system on July 15, 2015, 00:52:37&#160;UTC,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> after 22 hours of planned <a href="/wiki/Radio_silence#Non-military_radio_silence" title="Radio silence">radio silence</a> due to the spacecraft being pointed toward the Pluto system. Mission managers estimated a one in 10,000 chance that debris could have destroyed it during the flyby, preventing it from sending data to Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> The first details of the encounter were received the next day, but the download of the complete data set through the 2 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kilobit_per_second" title="Kilobit per second">kbps</a> data downlink took just over 15 months,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20161028_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20161028-19">[19]</a></sup> and analysis of the data will take longer.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Objectives">Objectives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Objectives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The mission\'s science objectives are grouped in three distinct priorities. The "primary objectives" were required; the "secondary objectives" were expected to be met but were not demanded. The "tertiary objectives" were desired. These objectives could have been skipped in favor of the above objectives. An objective to measure any magnetic field of Pluto was dropped. A <a href="/wiki/Magnetometer" title="Magnetometer">magnetometer</a> instrument could not be implemented within a reasonable mass budget and schedule, and <a href="#Science_payload">SWAP and PEPSSI</a> could do an indirect job detecting some magnetic field around Pluto.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li><b>Primary objectives (required)</b>\n<ul><li>Characterize the global geology and morphology of Pluto and Charon</li>\n<li>Map chemical compositions of Pluto and Charon surfaces</li>\n<li>Characterize the neutral (non-<a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ionized</a>) <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Pluto" title="Atmosphere of Pluto">atmosphere of Pluto</a> and its escape rate</li></ul></li>\n<li><b>Secondary objectives (expected)</b>\n<ul><li>Characterize the time variability of Pluto\'s surface and atmosphere</li>\n<li>Image select Pluto and Charon areas in <a href="/wiki/Stereoscopy" title="Stereoscopy">stereo</a></li>\n<li>Map the <a href="/wiki/Terminator_(solar)" title="Terminator (solar)">terminators</a> (day/night border) of Pluto and Charon with high resolution</li>\n<li>Map the chemical compositions of select Pluto and Charon areas with high resolution</li>\n<li>Characterize Pluto\'s <a href="/wiki/Ionosphere" title="Ionosphere">ionosphere</a> (upper layer of the atmosphere) and its interaction with the <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a></li>\n<li>Search for neutral species such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molecular_hydrogen" title="Molecular hydrogen">molecular hydrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_cyanide" title="Hydrogen cyanide">hydrogen cyanide</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Nitrile" title="Nitrile">nitriles</a> in the atmosphere</li>\n<li>Search for any Charon atmosphere</li>\n<li>Determine <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bolometric" title="Bolometric">bolometric</a> <a href="/wiki/Bond_albedo" title="Bond albedo">Bond albedos</a> for Pluto and Charon</li>\n<li>Map surface temperatures of Pluto and Charon</li>\n<li>Map any additional surfaces of outermost moons: <a href="/wiki/Nix_(moon)" title="Nix (moon)">Nix</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydra_(moon)" title="Hydra (moon)">Hydra</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kerberos_(moon)" title="Kerberos (moon)">Kerberos</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Styx_(moon)" title="Styx (moon)">Styx</a></li></ul></li>\n<li><b>Tertiary objectives (desired)</b>\n<ul><li>Characterize the energetic particle environment at Pluto and Charon</li>\n<li>Refine bulk parameters (radii, masses) and orbits of Pluto and Charon</li>\n<li>Search for additional <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">moons</a> and any <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planetary_ring" title="Planetary ring">rings</a></li></ul></li></ul>\n<p>"The New Horizons flyby of the Pluto system was fully successful, meeting and in many cases exceeding, the Pluto objectives set out for it by NASA and the National Academy of Sciences."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flyby_details">Flyby details</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Flyby details">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;max-width:292px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:144px;max-width:144px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:142px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pluto_by_LORRI_and_Ralph,_13_July_2015.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="700" data-file-width="700" decoding="async" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Pluto_by_LORRI_and_Ralph%2C_13_July_2015.jpg/142px-Pluto_by_LORRI_and_Ralph%2C_13_July_2015.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Pluto_by_LORRI_and_Ralph%2C_13_July_2015.jpg/213px-Pluto_by_LORRI_and_Ralph%2C_13_July_2015.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Pluto_by_LORRI_and_Ralph%2C_13_July_2015.jpg/284px-Pluto_by_LORRI_and_Ralph%2C_13_July_2015.jpg 2x" width="142"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Pluto\'s "encounter hemisphere" viewed by <i>New Horizons</i> on July 13, 2015</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:144px;max-width:144px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:142px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pluto_by_LORRI,_11_July_2015.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="599" data-file-width="599" decoding="async" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Pluto_by_LORRI%2C_11_July_2015.jpg/213px-Pluto_by_LORRI%2C_11_July_2015.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Pluto_by_LORRI%2C_11_July_2015.jpg/284px-Pluto_by_LORRI%2C_11_July_2015.jpg 2x" width="142"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Pluto\'s Charon-facing opposing hemisphere viewed on July 11, 2015</div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_0" style="width:220px;" videopayload=\'&lt;div class="mediaContainer" style="width:854px"&gt;&lt;video id="mwe_player_1" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/New_Horizons_flyby_of_Pluto_%28Eyes_on_the_Solar_System%29.ogv/854px--New_Horizons_flyby_of_Pluto_%28Eyes_on_the_Solar_System%29.ogv.jpg" Horizons flyby of Pluto (Eyes on the Solar System).ogv" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/New_Horizons_flyby_of_Pluto_%28Eyes_on_the_Solar_System%29.ogv/220px--New_Horizons_flyby_of_Pluto_%28Eyes_on_the_Solar_System%29.ogv.jpg" style="width:220px;height:124px"/><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/bd/New_Horizons_flyby_of_Pluto_%28Eyes_on_the_Solar_System%29.ogv" target="new" title="Play media"><span class="play-btn-large"><span class="mw-tmh-playtext">Play media</span></span></a></div> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:New_Horizons_flyby_of_Pluto_(Eyes_on_the_Solar_System).ogv" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Animation of <i>New Horizons</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'</span> flyby of Pluto in <a href="/wiki/NASA%27s_Eyes" title="NASA\'s Eyes">Eyes on the Solar System</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p><i>New Horizons</i> passed within 12,500&#160;km (7,800&#160;mi) of Pluto, with this closest approach on July 14, 2015, at 11:50&#160;UTC. <i>New Horizons</i> had a relative velocity of 13.78&#160;km/s (49,600&#160;km/h; 30,800&#160;mph) at its closest approach, and came as close as 28,800&#160;km (17,900&#160;mi) to Charon. Starting 3.2 days before the closest approach, long-range imaging included the mapping of Pluto and Charon to 40&#160;km (25&#160;mi) resolution. This is half the rotation period of the Pluto&#8211;Charon system and allowed imaging of all sides of both bodies. Close range imaging was repeated twice per day in order to search for surface changes caused by localized snow fall or surface <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cryovolcanism" title="Cryovolcanism">cryovolcanism</a>. Because of Pluto\'s tilt, a portion of the northern hemisphere would be in shadow at all times. During the flyby, engineers expected LORRI to be able to obtain select images with resolution as high as 50&#160;m per pixel (160&#160;ft/px) if closest distance were around 12,500&#160;km, and MVIC was expected to obtain four-color global dayside maps at 1.6&#160;km (1&#160;mi) resolution. LORRI and MVIC attempted to overlap their respective coverage areas to form stereo pairs. LEISA obtained hyperspectral near-infrared maps at 7&#160;km/px (4.3&#160;mi/px) globally and 0.6&#160;km/px (0.37&#160;mi/px) for selected areas.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Snakeskin_terrain_on_Pluto_(PIA19957).png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="1558" decoding="async" height="136" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Snakeskin_terrain_on_Pluto_%28PIA19957%29.png/220px-Snakeskin_terrain_on_Pluto_%28PIA19957%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Snakeskin_terrain_on_Pluto_%28PIA19957%29.png/330px-Snakeskin_terrain_on_Pluto_%28PIA19957%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Snakeskin_terrain_on_Pluto_%28PIA19957%29.png/440px-Snakeskin_terrain_on_Pluto_%28PIA19957%29.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Snakeskin_terrain_on_Pluto_(PIA19957).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Patterns of blue-gray ridges and reddish material observed in the Tartarus Dorsa region on July 14, 2015</div></div></div>\n<p>Meanwhile, Alice characterized the atmosphere, both by emissions of atmospheric molecules (<a href="/wiki/Airglow" title="Airglow">airglow</a>), and by dimming of background stars as they pass behind Pluto (<a href="/wiki/Occultation" title="Occultation">occultation</a>). During and after closest approach, SWAP and PEPSSI sampled the high atmosphere and its effects on the solar wind. VBSDC searched for dust, inferring meteoroid collision rates and any invisible rings. REX performed active and passive radio science. The communications dish on Earth measured the disappearance and reappearance of the <a href="/wiki/Radio_occultation" title="Radio occultation">radio occultation</a> signal as the probe flew by behind Pluto. The results resolved Pluto\'s diameter (by their timing) and atmospheric density and composition (by their weakening and strengthening pattern). (Alice can perform similar occultations, using sunlight instead of radio beacons.) Previous missions had the spacecraft transmit through the atmosphere, to Earth ("downlink"). Pluto\'s mass and mass distribution were evaluated by the gravitational tug on the spacecraft. As the spacecraft speeds up and slows down, the radio signal exhibited a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doppler_shift" title="Doppler shift">Doppler shift</a>. The Doppler shift was measured by comparison with the ultrastable oscillator in the communications electronics.\n</p><p>Reflected sunlight from Charon allowed some imaging observations of the nightside. Backlighting by the Sun gave an opportunity to highlight any rings or atmospheric hazes. REX performed radiometry of the nightside.\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Satellite_observations">Satellite observations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Satellite observations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><i>New Horizons</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'</span> best spatial resolution of the small satellites is 330&#160;m per pixel (1,080&#160;ft/px) at Nix, 780&#160;m/px (2,560&#160;ft/px) at Hydra, and approximately 1.8&#160;km/px (1.1&#160;mi/px) at Kerberos and Styx. Estimates for the diameters of these bodies are: Nix at 54&#160;&#215;&#160;41&#160;&#215;&#160;36&#160;km (34&#160;&#215;&#160;25&#160;&#215;&#160;22&#160;mi); Hydra at 43&#160;&#215;&#160;33&#160;km (27&#160;&#215;&#160;21&#160;mi); Kerberos at 12&#160;&#215;&#160;4.5&#160;km (7.5&#160;&#215;&#160;2.8&#160;mi); and Styx at 7&#160;&#215;&#160;5&#160;km (4.3&#160;&#215;&#160;3.1&#160;mi). This translates to a resolution of 164/124/109, 55/42/?, 7/3/?, and 4/3/? pixels in width for Nix, Hydra, Kerberos, and Styx, respectively.\n</p><p>Initial predictions envisioned Kerberos as a relatively large and massive object whose dark surface led to it having a faint reflection. This proved to be wrong as images obtained by <i>New Horizons</i> on July 14 and sent back to Earth in October 2015 revealed an object just 8&#160;km (5.0&#160;mi) across with a highly reflective surface suggesting the presence of relatively clean water ice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kerberos_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-kerberos-143">[143]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tnone center"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:791px;max-width:791px"><div style="clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;background-color:transparent">Satellites of Pluto imaged by <i>New Horizons</i></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:162px;max-width:162px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:160px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Charon_in_Color_(HQ).jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="900" data-file-width="900" decoding="async" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Charon_in_Color_%28HQ%29.jpg/160px-Charon_in_Color_%28HQ%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Charon_in_Color_%28HQ%29.jpg/240px-Charon_in_Color_%28HQ%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Charon_in_Color_%28HQ%29.jpg/320px-Charon_in_Color_%28HQ%29.jpg 2x" width="160"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Charon_(moon)" title="Charon (moon)">Charon</a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:146px;max-width:146px"><div class="thumbimage"
