src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Nix_best_view.jpg/144px-Nix_best_view.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Nix_best_view.jpg/216px-Nix_best_view.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Nix_best_view.jpg/288px-Nix_best_view.jpg 2x" width="144"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Nix_(moon)" title="Nix (moon)">Nix</a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:162px;max-width:162px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:160px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HydraHires.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="450" data-file-width="450" decoding="async" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/HydraHires.jpg/160px-HydraHires.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/HydraHires.jpg/240px-HydraHires.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/HydraHires.jpg/320px-HydraHires.jpg 2x" width="160"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Hydra_(moon)" title="Hydra (moon)">Hydra</a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:162px;max-width:162px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:160px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kerberos_(moon).jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1400" data-file-width="1400" decoding="async" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Kerberos_%28moon%29.jpg/160px-Kerberos_%28moon%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Kerberos_%28moon%29.jpg/240px-Kerberos_%28moon%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Kerberos_%28moon%29.jpg/320px-Kerberos_%28moon%29.jpg 2x" width="160"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Kerberos_(moon)" title="Kerberos (moon)">Kerberos</a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:149px;max-width:149px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:160px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Styx_(moon).jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="328" data-file-width="300" decoding="async" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Styx_%28moon%29.jpg/147px-Styx_%28moon%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Styx_%28moon%29.jpg/221px-Styx_%28moon%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Styx_%28moon%29.jpg/294px-Styx_%28moon%29.jpg 2x" width="147"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Styx_(moon)" title="Styx (moon)">Styx</a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Commons-logo.svg"><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="1376" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4a/Commons-logo.svg/12px-Commons-logo.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4a/Commons-logo.svg/18px-Commons-logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4a/Commons-logo.svg/24px-Commons-logo.svg.png 2x" width="12"/></a> Media related to <a class="extiw" href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/Category:Photos_of_Pluto_system_by_New_Horizons" title="commons:Category:Photos of Pluto system by New Horizons">Photos of Pluto system by New Horizons</a> at Wikimedia Commons</div></div></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-Pluto_events">Post-Pluto events</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Post-Pluto events">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Blue_hazes_over_backlit_Pluto.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Blue_hazes_over_backlit_Pluto.jpg/220px-Blue_hazes_over_backlit_Pluto.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Blue_hazes_over_backlit_Pluto.jpg/330px-Blue_hazes_over_backlit_Pluto.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Blue_hazes_over_backlit_Pluto.jpg/440px-Blue_hazes_over_backlit_Pluto.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Blue_hazes_over_backlit_Pluto.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>View of Pluto as <i>New Horizons</i> left the system, catching the Sun\'s rays passing through Pluto\'s atmosphere, forming a ring</div></div></div>\n<p>Soon after the Pluto flyby, in July 2015, <i>New Horizons</i> reported that the spacecraft was healthy, its flight path was within the margins, and science data of the Pluto&#8211;Charon system had been recorded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20150714-kc2_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20150714-kc2-144">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> The spacecraft\'s immediate task was to begin returning the 6.25 gigabytes of information collected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20161028_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20161028-19">[19]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Free-space_path_loss" title="Free-space path loss">free-space path loss</a> at its distance of 4.5 light-hours (3,000,000,000&#160;km) is approximately 303 <a href="/wiki/Decibel" title="Decibel">dB</a> at 7&#160;GHz. Using the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_gain_antenna" title="High gain antenna">high gain antenna</a> and transmitting at full power, the signal from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/EIRP" title="EIRP">EIRP</a> is +83 dBm, and at this distance the signal reaching Earth is &#8722;220 dBm. The received signal level (RSL) using one, un-arrayed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deep_Space_Network" title="Deep Space Network">Deep Space Network</a> antenna with 72 dBi of forward gain equals &#8722;148 dBm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup> Because of the extremely low RSL, it could only transmit data at 1 to 2 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Data_rate_units#Kilobit_per_second" title="Data rate units">kilobits per second</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jhuhub20150717_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-jhuhub20150717-147">[147]</a></sup>\n</p><p>By March 30, 2016, <i>New Horizons</i> had reached the halfway point of transmitting this data.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-airspace20160330_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-airspace20160330-148">[148]</a></sup> The transfer was completed on October 25, 2016 at 21:48&#160;UTC, when the last piece of data&#8212;part of a Pluto&#8211;Charon observation sequence by the Ralph/LEISA imager&#8212;was received by the Johns Hopkins University <a href="/wiki/Applied_Physics_Laboratory" title="Applied Physics Laboratory">Applied Physics Laboratory</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20161028_19-3"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20161028-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa20161027_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa20161027-149">[149]</a></sup>\n</p><p>At a distance of 43&#160;AU (6.43&#160;billion&#160;km; 4.00&#160;billion&#160;mi) from the Sun and 0.4&#160;AU (60&#160;million&#160;km; 37&#160;million&#160;mi) from <span class="nowrap">2014 MU<sub>69</sub></span> as of November 2018,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-currentposition_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-currentposition-150">[150]</a></sup> <i>New Horizons</i> is <a class="extiw" href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/File:New_Horizons_Full_Trajectory.svg" title="commons:File:New Horizons Full Trajectory.svg">heading in the direction</a> of the constellation <a href="/wiki/Sagittarius_(constellation)" title="Sagittarius (constellation)">Sagittarius</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-heavens-escaping_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-heavens-escaping-151">[151]</a></sup> at 14.10&#160;<a href="/wiki/Metre_per_second" title="Metre per second">km/s</a> (8.76&#160;<a href="/wiki/Mile" title="Mile">mi/s</a>; 2.97&#160;<a href="/wiki/Speed" title="Speed">AU/a</a>) relative to the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-currentposition_150-1"><a href="#cite_note-currentposition-150">[150]</a></sup> The brightness of the Sun from the spacecraft is <a href="/wiki/Magnitude_(astronomy)" title="Magnitude (astronomy)">magnitude</a> &#8722;18.5.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-heavens-escaping_151-1"><a href="#cite_note-heavens-escaping-151">[151]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mission_extension">Mission extension</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Mission extension">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Oort_cloud_Sedna_orbit.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2499" data-file-width="2499" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Oort_cloud_Sedna_orbit.svg/220px-Oort_cloud_Sedna_orbit.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Oort_cloud_Sedna_orbit.svg/330px-Oort_cloud_Sedna_orbit.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Oort_cloud_Sedna_orbit.svg/440px-Oort_cloud_Sedna_orbit.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Oort_cloud_Sedna_orbit.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Big picture: from the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System#Inner_Solar_System" title="Solar System">inner Solar System</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Oort_cloud" title="Oort cloud">Oort cloud</a> with the Kuiper belt in between</div></div></div>\n<p>The <i>New Horizons</i> team requested, and received, a mission extension through 2021 to explore additional Kuiper belt objects (KBOs). Funding was secured on July 1, 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mission_Extension_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mission_Extension-152">[152]</a></sup> During this Kuiper Belt Extended Mission (KEM), the spacecraft has performed a close fly-by of <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/(486958)_2014_MU69" title="(486958) 2014 MU69">(486958) 2014 MU<span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;"><span style="font-size:80%;">69</span></span></a></span> and will conduct more distant observations on an additional two dozen objects,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-extension_request_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-extension_request-153">[153]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mission_Extension_152-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mission_Extension-152">[152]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-verge20160701_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-verge20160701-154">[154]</a></sup> and possibly make a fly-by of another KBO.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kuiper_belt_object_mission">Kuiper belt object mission</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Kuiper belt object mission">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Target_background">Target background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Target background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Mission planners searched for one or more additional Kuiper belt objects (KBOs) of the order of 50&#8211;100&#160;km (31&#8211;62&#160;mi) in diameter as targets for flybys similar to the spacecraft\'s Plutonian encounter. However, despite the large population of KBOs, many factors limited the number of possible targets. Because the flight path was determined by the Pluto flyby, and the probe only had 33 kilograms of <a href="/wiki/Hydrazine" title="Hydrazine">hydrazine</a> <a href="/wiki/Rocket_propellant" title="Rocket propellant">propellant</a> remaining, the object to be visited needed to be within a cone of less than a degree\'s width extending from Pluto. The target also needed to be within 55<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>AU, because beyond 55<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>AU, the communications link will become too weak, and the RTG power output will have decayed significantly enough to hinder observations. Desirable KBOs would be well over 50&#160;km (30&#160;mi) in diameter, neutral in color (to contrast with the reddish Pluto), and, if possible, have a moon that imparts a wobble.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Search">Search</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Search">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>In 2011, mission scientists started a dedicated search for suitable KBOs using ground telescopes. Large ground telescopes with wide-field cameras, notably the twin 6.5-meter <a href="/wiki/Magellan_Telescopes" title="Magellan Telescopes">Magellan Telescopes</a> in Chile, the 8.2-meter <a href="/wiki/Subaru_Telescope" title="Subaru Telescope">Subaru Observatory</a> in Hawaii, and the <a href="/wiki/Canada%E2%80%93France%E2%80%93Hawaii_Telescope" title="Canada&#8211;France&#8211;Hawaii Telescope">Canada&#8211;France&#8211;Hawaii Telescope</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-parker2012_116-1"><a href="#cite_note-parker2012-116">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pluto-bound_probe_faces_crisis_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pluto-bound_probe_faces_crisis-155">[155]</a></sup> were used to search for potential targets. By participating in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Citizen-science" title="Citizen-science">citizen-science</a> project called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zooniverse_(citizen_science_project)#Ice_Hunters" title="Zooniverse (citizen science project)">Ice Hunters</a>, the public helped to scan telescopic images for possible suitable mission candidates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-icehunters_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-icehunters-156">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-icehuntersdone_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-icehuntersdone-157">[157]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasaice_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasaice-159">[159]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-exciting_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-exciting-160">[160]</a></sup> The ground-based search resulted in the discovery of about 143 KBOs of potential interest,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup> but none of these were close enough to the flight path of <i>New Horizons</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pluto-bound_probe_faces_crisis_155-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pluto-bound_probe_faces_crisis-155">[155]</a></sup> Only the <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble Space Telescope</a> was deemed likely to find a suitable target in time for a successful KBO mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-witze20140520_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-witze20140520-162">[162]</a></sup> On June 16, 2014, time on Hubble was granted for a search.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup> Hubble has a much greater ability to find suitable KBOs than ground telescopes. The probability that a target for <i>New Horizons</i> would be found was estimated beforehand at about 95%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Suitable_KBOs">Suitable KBOs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Suitable KBOs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:KBO_2014_MU69_HST.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1118" data-file-width="1118" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/KBO_2014_MU69_HST.jpg/220px-KBO_2014_MU69_HST.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/KBO_2014_MU69_HST.jpg/330px-KBO_2014_MU69_HST.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/KBO_2014_MU69_HST.jpg/440px-KBO_2014_MU69_HST.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:KBO_2014_MU69_HST.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/(486958)_2014_MU69" title="(486958) 2014 MU69">(486958) 2014 MU<span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;"><span style="font-size:80%;">69</span></span></a></span>, the announced target for the Kuiper belt object mission</div></div></div>\n<p>On October 15, 2014, it was revealed that Hubble\'s search had uncovered three potential targets,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20141015_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20141015-165">[165]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lakdawalla2014_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lakdawalla2014-166">[166]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wall2014_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wall2014-168">[168]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup> temporarily designated PT1 ("potential target 1"), PT2 and PT3 by the <i>New Horizons</i> team. All are objects with estimated diameters in the 30&#8211;55&#160;km (19&#8211;34&#160;mi) range and were too small to be seen by ground telescopes. Each were at distances from the Sun of ranging from 43 to 44 AU, which would put the encounters in the 2018&#8211;2019 period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lakdawalla2014_166-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lakdawalla2014-166">[166]</a></sup> The initial estimated probabilities that these objects were reachable within <i>New Horizons</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'</span> fuel budget are 100%, 7%, and 97%, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lakdawalla2014_166-2"><a href="#cite_note-Lakdawalla2014-166">[166]</a></sup> All are members of the "cold" (low-<a href="/wiki/Orbital_inclination" title="Orbital inclination">inclination</a>, low-<a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">eccentricity</a>) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Classical_Kuiper_belt" title="Classical Kuiper belt">classical Kuiper belt</a>, and thus are very different from Pluto.  <a href="/wiki/(486958)_2014_MU69" title="(486958) 2014 MU69">PT1</a> (given the temporary designation "1110113Y" on the HST web site<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup>), the most favorably situated object, has a magnitude of 26.8, is 30&#8211;45&#160;km (19&#8211;28&#160;mi) in diameter, and was encountered in January 2019.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VOX-20150414_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-VOX-20150414-171">[171]</a></sup> A course change to reach it required about 35% of <i>New Horizons</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'</span> available <a href="/wiki/Delta-v_budget" title="Delta-v budget">trajectory-adjustment fuel supply</a>.  A mission to PT3 was in some ways preferable, in that it is brighter and therefore probably larger than PT1, but the greater fuel requirements to reach it would have left less for maneuvering and unforeseen events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lakdawalla2014_166-3"><a href="#cite_note-Lakdawalla2014-166">[166]</a></sup> Once sufficient orbital information was provided, the <a href="/wiki/Minor_Planet_Center" title="Minor Planet Center">Minor Planet Center</a> gave <a href="/wiki/Provisional_designation_in_astronomy" title="Provisional designation in astronomy">provisional designations</a> to the three target KBOs: <span class="nowrap"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2014_MU69" title="2014 MU69">2014 MU<span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;"><span style="font-size:80%;">69</span></span></a></span> (PT1), <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/2014_OS393" title="2014 OS393">2014 OS<span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;"><span style="font-size:80%;">393</span></span></a></span> (PT2), and <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/2014_PN70" title="2014 PN70">2014 PN<span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;"><span style="font-size:80%;">70</span></span></a></span> (PT3). By the fall of 2014, a possible fourth target, <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/2014_MT69" title="2014 MT69">2014 MT<span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;"><span style="font-size:80%;">69</span></span></a></span>, had been eliminated by follow-up observations. PT2 was out of the running before the Pluto flyby.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup>  The spacecraft will also study almost 20 KBOs from afar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="KBO_selection">KBO selection</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: KBO selection">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>On August 28, 2015, <span class="nowrap">(486958) 2014 MU<sub>69</sub></span> (PT1) was chosen as the flyby target. The necessary course adjustment was performed with four engine firings between October 22 and November 4, 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wired-20151104_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-wired-20151104-175">[175]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup> The flyby occurred on January 1, 2019, at 00:33 UTC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Observations_of_other_KBOs">Observations of other KBOs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Observations of other KBOs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Aside from its flyby of <span class="nowrap">(486958) 2014 MU<sub>69</sub></span>, the extended mission for <i>New Horizons</i> calls for the spacecraft to conduct observations of, and look for ring systems around, between 25 and 35 different KBOs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Porter2017_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-Porter2017-179">[179]</a></sup> In addition, it will continue to study the gas, dust and plasma composition of the Kuiper belt before the mission extension ends in 2021.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-extension_request_153-1"><a href="#cite_note-extension_request-153">[153]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-verge20160701_154-1"><a href="#cite_note-verge20160701-154">[154]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On November 2, 2015, <i>New Horizons</i> imaged KBO <a href="/wiki/15810_Arawn" title="15810 Arawn">15810 Arawn</a> with the LORRI instrument from 280&#160;million&#160;km away (170&#160;million&#160;mi; 1.9&#160;AU), showing the shape of the object and one or two details.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spdaily20151207_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-spdaily20151207-180">[180]</a></sup> This KBO was again imaged by the LORRI instrument on April 7&#8211;8, 2016, from a distance of 111&#160;million&#160;km (69&#160;million&#160;mi; 0.74&#160;AU). The new images allowed the science team to further refine the location of 15810 Arawn to within 1,000&#160;km (620&#160;mi) and to determine its rotational period of 5.47 hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unitoday20160613_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-unitoday20160613-181">[181]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jhuapl20160518_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-jhuapl20160518-182">[182]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In July 2016, the LORRI camera captured some distant images of <a href="/wiki/50000_Quaoar" title="50000 Quaoar">Quaoar</a> from 2.1&#160;billion&#160;km away (1.3&#160;billion&#160;mi; 14&#160;AU); the oblique view will complement Earth-based observations to study the object\'s light-scattering properties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa20160831_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa20160831-183">[183]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On December 5, 2017, when <i>New Horizons</i> was 40.9&#160;AU from Earth, a calibration image of the <a href="/wiki/NGC_3532" title="NGC 3532">Wishing Well cluster</a> marked the most distant image from Earth ever taken by a spacecraft (breaking the 27-year record set by <i><a href="/wiki/Voyager_1" title="Voyager 1">Voyager 1</a></i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'</span>s famous <i><a href="/wiki/Pale_Blue_Dot" title="Pale Blue Dot">Pale Blue Dot</a></i>). Two hours later, <i>New Horizons</i> surpassed its own record, imaging the Kuiper belt objects <span class="nowrap">2012 HZ<sub>84</sub></span> and <span class="nowrap">2012 HE<sub>85</sub></span> from a distance of 0.50 and 0.34&#160;AU, respectively. These are the closest images taken of a Kuiper belt object besides Pluto and <span class="nowrap">2014 MU<sub>69</sub></span> as of  February&#160;2018<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa20180208_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa20180208-184">[184]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tnone center"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:793px;max-width:793px"><div objects by New Horizons">Photos of Kuiper belt objects by <i>New Horizons</i></a> at Wikimedia Commons</div></div></div>\n<h2><span id="Encounter_with_.28486958.29_2014_MU69"></span><span class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Animation_of_New_Horizons_trajectory.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Animation of <i>New Horizons</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> trajectory from January 19, 2006 to  December 30, 2030<br/> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:magenta; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160; <i>New Horizons</i> </span>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:lime; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:Royalblue; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a></span>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:Gold; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/132524_APL" title="132524 APL">132524 APL</a></span>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:OrangeRed; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a> </span>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:Cyan; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a> </span></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2014MU69_MVIC.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/2014MU69_MVIC.png/220px-2014MU69_MVIC.png" href="/wiki/File:2014MU69_MVIC.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>New Horizons</i> image of <span class="nowrap">2014 MU<sub>69</sub></span>. The larger body, at the right, is nicknamed "Ultima", and the smaller "Thule".</div></div></div><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/(486958)_2014_MU69#Exploration" title="(486958) 2014 MU69">(486958) 2014 MU69 &#167;&#160;Exploration</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Objectives_2">Objectives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Objectives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Science objectives of the flyby included characterizing the geology and morphology of <span class="nowrap"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2014_MU69" title="2014 MU69">2014 MU<span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;"><span style="font-size:80%;">69</span></span></a></span> (nicknamed "Ultima Thule"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20181231_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20181231-186">[186]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20181230_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20181230-187">[187]</a></sup>  and mapping the surface composition (by searching for ammonia, carbon monoxide, methane, and water ice). Searches will be conducted for orbiting moonlets, a coma, rings, and the surrounding environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2017_AGU_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-2017_AGU-188">[188]</a></sup> Additional objectives include:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-skytel20151001_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-skytel20151001-189">[189]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li>Mapping the surface geology to learn how it formed and evolved</li>\n<li>Measuring the surface temperature</li>\n<li>Mapping the 3-D surface topography and surface composition to learn how it is similar to and different from comets such as <a href="/wiki/67P/Churyumov%E2%80%93Gerasimenko" title="67P/Churyumov&#8211;Gerasimenko">67P/Churyumov&#8211;Gerasimenko</a> and dwarf planets such as Pluto</li>\n<li>Searching for any signs of activity, such as a cloud-like coma</li>\n<li>Searching for, and studying, any satellites or rings</li>\n<li>Measuring or constraining the mass</li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Targeting_maneuvers">Targeting maneuvers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Targeting maneuvers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><span class="nowrap">2014 MU<sub>69</sub></span> is the first object to be targeted for a flyby that was discovered after the spacecraft was launched.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lakdawalla20150901_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lakdawalla20150901-190">[190]</a></sup> <i>New Horizons</i> is planned to come within 3,500&#160;km (2,200&#160;mi) of <span class="nowrap">2014 MU<sub>69</sub></span>, three times closer than the spacecraft\'s earlier encounter with Pluto. Images with a resolution of up to 30&#160;m (98&#160;ft) per pixel are expected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The new mission began on October 22, 2015, when <i>New Horizons</i> carried out the first in a series of four initial targeting maneuvers designed to send it toward <span class="nowrap">2014 MU<sub>69</sub></span>. The maneuver, which started at approximately 19:50&#160;UTC and used two of the spacecraft\'s small hydrazine-fueled thrusters, lasted approximately 16 minutes and changed the spacecraft\'s trajectory by about 10 meters per second (33&#160;ft/s). The remaining three targeting maneuvers took place on October 25, October 28, and November 4, 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spdaily20151106_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-spdaily20151106-192">[192]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jhuapl20151029_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-jhuapl20151029-193">[193]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Approach_phase">Approach phase</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Approach phase">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The craft was brought out of its hibernation at approximately 00:33&#160;<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UTC" title="UTC">UTC</a> <a href="/wiki/Spacecraft_Event_Time" title="Spacecraft Event Time"><abbr title="Spacecraft Event Time">SCET</abbr></a> on June 5, 2018 (06:12&#160;UTC <a href="/wiki/Spacecraft_Event_Time" title="Spacecraft Event Time">ERT</a>, Earth-Received Time),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-note_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-note-195">[a]</a></sup> in order to prepare for the approach phase.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-verge20180605_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-verge20180605-196">[195]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jhuapl20180605_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-jhuapl20180605-197">[196]</a></sup> After verifying its health status, the spacecraft transitioned from a <a href="/wiki/Spin-stabilisation" title="Spin-stabilisation">spin-stabilized</a> mode to a three-axis-stabilized mode on August 13, 2018. The official approach phase began on August 16, 2018, and continued through December 24, 2018.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Planetary_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-Planetary-198">[197]</a></sup> The first distant images from <i>New Horizons</i> were acquired starting in early September 2018.\n</p><p><i>New Horizons</i> made its first detection of <span class="nowrap">2014 MU<sub>69</sub></span> on August 16, 2018, from a distance of 107&#160;million&#160;mi (172&#160;million&#160;km). At that time, <span class="nowrap">2014 MU<sub>69</sub></span> was visible at magnitude 20, against a crowded stellar background in the direction of the constellation <a href="/wiki/Sagittarius_(constellation)" title="Sagittarius (constellation)">Sagittarius</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-First_detection_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-First_detection-199">[198]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JPLHorizonsX_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-JPLHorizonsX-200">[199]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Core_phase">Core phase</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Core phase">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The Core phase began a week before the encounter, and continued for two days after the encounter. The spacecraft flew by the object at a speed of 51,500&#160;km/h (32,000&#160;mph; 14.3&#160;km/s) and within 3,500&#160;km (2,200&#160;mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[200]</a></sup> The majority of the science data was collected within 48 hours of the closest approach in a phase called the Inner Core.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Planetary_198-1"><a href="#cite_note-Planetary-198">[197]</a></sup> Closest approach occurred January 1, 2019, at 05:33&#160;<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UTC" title="UTC">UTC</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sfnow20180106_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-sfnow20180106-202">[201]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Spacecraft_Event_Time" title="Spacecraft Event Time">SCET</a> at which point it was <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7012649254758838000&#9824;</span>43.4&#160;<a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">AU</a></span> from the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[202]</a></sup> At this distance, the one-way transit time for radio signals between Earth and <i>New Horizons</i> was six hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2017_AGU_188-1"><a href="#cite_note-2017_AGU-188">[188]</a></sup> Confirmation that the craft had succeeded in filling its digital recorders with data only arrived on Earth ten hours later, at 15:29 UTC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[203]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Data_download">Data download</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Data download">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>After the encounter, preliminary, high-priority data was sent to Earth on January 1 and 2, 2019. On January 9, <i>New Horizons</i> returned to a spin-stabilized mode, to prepare to send the remainder of its data back to Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Planetary_198-2"><a href="#cite_note-Planetary-198">[197]</a></sup> This download is expected to take 20 months at a data rate of 1&#8211;2 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kilobit_per_second" title="Kilobit per second">kilobits per second</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[204]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Post_2014_MU69_encounter_events">Post <span class="nowrap">2014 MU<sub>69</sub></span> encounter events</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Post 2014 MU69 encounter events">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:New_Horizons_Full_Trajectory_Sideview.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="251" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="108" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/New_Horizons_Full_Trajectory_Sideview.svg/220px-New_Horizons_Full_Trajectory_Sideview.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/New_Horizons_Full_Trajectory_Sideview.svg/330px-New_Horizons_Full_Trajectory_Sideview.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/New_Horizons_Full_Trajectory_Sideview.svg/440px-New_Horizons_Full_Trajectory_Sideview.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:New_Horizons_Full_Trajectory_Sideview.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>New Horizons</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'</span>  position as of December 2018<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-currentposition_150-2"><a href="#cite_note-currentposition-150">[150]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>After the spacecraft\'s passage by <span class="nowrap">2014 MU<sub>69</sub></span>, the instrument continues to have sufficient power to be operational until the 2030s. Team leader Alan Stern stated the potential for a third flyby in the 2020s at the outer edges of the Kuiper belt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[205]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[206]</a></sup> This depends on a suitable Kuiper belt object still to be found or confirmed close enough to the spacecraft\'s current trajectory.\n</p><p>In addition, <i>New Horizons</i> may take a picture of Earth from its distance in the Kuiper belt, but only after completing all planned KBO flybys.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[207]</a></sup> This is because pointing a camera towards Earth risks it being damaged by sunlight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[208]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Speed">Speed</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: Speed">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:New_Horizons_Speed_and_distance_from_Sun.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="535" data-file-width="659" decoding="async" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/New_Horizons_Speed_and_distance_from_Sun.svg/220px-New_Horizons_Speed_and_distance_from_Sun.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/New_Horizons_Speed_and_distance_from_Sun.svg/330px-New_Horizons_Speed_and_distance_from_Sun.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/New_Horizons_Speed_and_distance_from_Sun.svg/440px-New_Horizons_Speed_and_distance_from_Sun.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:New_Horizons_Speed_and_distance_from_Sun.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Speed and distance from the Sun</div></div></div>\n<p><i>New Horizons</i> has been called "the fastest spacecraft ever launched"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-APL-20070116_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-APL-20070116-10">[10]</a></sup> because it left Earth at 16.26 kilometers per second (58,536&#160;km/h; 36,373&#160;mph), faster than any other spacecraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sciam20130225_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-sciam20130225-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ionine20150609_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-ionine20150609-12">[12]</a></sup> It is also the first spacecraft launched directly into a solar escape trajectory, which requires an approximate speed while near Earth of 16.5&#160;km/s (59,000&#160;km/h; 37,000&#160;mph),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[b]</a></sup> plus additional <a href="/wiki/Delta-v" title="Delta-v">delta-v</a> to cover <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Air_drag" title="Air drag">air</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gravity_drag" title="Gravity drag">gravity drag</a>, all to be provided by the launch vehicle.\n</p><p>However, it is not the fastest spacecraft to leave the Solar System. As of  January&#160;2018<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, this record is held by <i>Voyager 1</i>, traveling at 16.985&#160;km/s (61,146&#160;km/h; 37,994&#160;mph) relative to the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-heavens-escaping_151-2"><a href="#cite_note-heavens-escaping-151">[151]</a></sup> <i>Voyager 1</i> attained greater <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hyperbolic_excess_velocity" title="Hyperbolic excess velocity">hyperbolic excess velocity</a> than <i>New Horizons</i> thanks to <a href="/wiki/Gravity_assist" title="Gravity assist">gravity assists</a> by Jupiter and Saturn.  When <i>New Horizons</i> reaches the distance of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7013149597870700000&#9824;</span>100&#160;<a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">AU</a></span>, it will be travelling at about 13&#160;km/s (47,000&#160;km/h; 29,000&#160;mph), around 4&#160;km/s (14,000&#160;km/h; 8,900&#160;mph) slower than <i>Voyager 1</i> at that distance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jhuapl2006_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-jhuapl2006-211">[209]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Parker_Solar_Probe" title="Parker Solar Probe">Parker Solar Probe</a> can also be measured as the fastest object, because of its <a href="/wiki/Orbital_speed" title="Orbital speed">orbital speed</a> relative to the Sun at <a href="/wiki/Apsis" title="Apsis">perihelion</a>: 95.3&#160;km/s (343,000&#160;km/h; 213,000&#160;mph).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-parker-note_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-parker-note-212">[c]</a></sup> Because it remains in solar orbit, its <a href="/wiki/Specific_orbital_energy" title="Specific orbital energy">specific orbital energy</a> relative to the Sun is lower than <i>New Horizons</i> and other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_artificial_objects_escaping_from_the_Solar_System" title="List of artificial objects escaping from the Solar System">artificial objects escaping the Solar System</a>.\n</p>\n<p><i>New Horizons</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'</span> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Star_48B" title="Star 48B">Star 48B</a> third stage is also on a <a href="/wiki/Hyperbolic_trajectory" title="Hyperbolic trajectory">hyperbolic escape trajectory</a> from the Solar System, and reached Jupiter before the <i>New Horizons</i> spacecraft; it was expected to cross Pluto\'s orbit on October 15, 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-star48_213-0"><a href="#cite_note-star48-213">[210]</a></sup> Because it is not in controlled flight, it did not receive the correct gravity assist, and passed within 200&#160;million&#160;km (120&#160;million&#160;mi) of Pluto.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-star48_213-1"><a href="#cite_note-star48-213">[210]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Centaur_(rocket_stage)" title="Centaur (rocket stage)">Centaur</a> second stage did not achieve solar escape velocity, and remains in a heliocentric orbit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[211]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-parker-note_212-1"><a href="#cite_note-parker-note-212">[c]</a></sup>\n</p><div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Gallery">Gallery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=52" title="Edit section: Gallery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Images_of_the_launch">Images of the launch</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span <a href="/wiki/File:15-02652-PlutoFilm-50sec-20150714.webm" title="File:15-02652-PlutoFilm-50sec-20150714.webm">released December 5, 2015</a>)</div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Timeline">Timeline</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=55" title="Edit section: Timeline">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Preparation_phase">Preparation phase</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=56" title="Edit section: Preparation phase">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<ul><li>January 8, 2001: Proposal team meets face-to-face for the first time at the Johns Hopkins University Applied Physics Laboratory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stern2006_216-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stern2006-216">[213]</a></sup></li>\n<li>February 5, 2001: <i>New Horizons</i> name chosen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stern2006_216-1"><a href="#cite_note-Stern2006-216">[213]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stern2005_217-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stern2005-217">[214]</a></sup></li>\n<li>April 6, 2001: <i>New Horizons</i> proposal submitted to NASA. It was one of five proposals submitted, which were later narrowed to two for Phase&#160;A study: POSSE (Pluto and Outer Solar System Explorer) and <i>New Horizons</i>. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stern2006_216-2"><a href="#cite_note-Stern2006-216">[213]</a></sup></li>\n<li>November 29, 2001: <i>New Horizons</i> proposal selected by NASA. Started Phase&#160;B study. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Savage_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-Savage-218">[215]</a></sup></li>\n<li>March 2002: Budget zeroed by Bush administration, later overridden.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[216]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[217]</a></sup></li>\n<li>June 13, 2005: Spacecraft departed <a href="/wiki/Applied_Physics_Laboratory" title="Applied Physics Laboratory">Applied Physics Laboratory</a> for final testing. It undergoes final testing at <a href="/wiki/Goddard_Space_Flight_Center" title="Goddard Space Flight Center">Goddard Space Flight Center</a> (GSFC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[218]</a></sup></li>\n<li>September 24, 2005: Spacecraft shipped to <a href="/wiki/Cape_Canaveral" title="Cape Canaveral">Cape Canaveral</a>. It was moved through <a href="/wiki/Andrews_Air_Force_Base" title="Andrews Air Force Base">Andrews Air Force Base</a> aboard a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/C-17_Globemaster_III" title="C-17 Globemaster III">C-17 Globemaster III</a> <a href="/wiki/Cargo_aircraft" title="Cargo aircraft">cargo aircraft</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SpaceDaily_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-SpaceDaily-78">[78]</a></sup></li>\n<li>December 17, 2005: Spacecraft ready for in rocket positioning. Transported from Hazardous Servicing Facility to Vertical Integration Facility at Space Launch Complex 41.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[219]</a></sup></li>\n<li>January 11, 2006: Primary launch window opened. The launch was delayed for further testing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LaunchPrep1_223-0"><a href="#cite_note-LaunchPrep1-223">[220]</a></sup></li>\n<li>January 16, 2006: Rocket moved onto launch pad. <a href="/wiki/Atlas_V" title="Atlas V">Atlas V</a> launcher, serial number AV-010, rolled out onto pad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20150306_224-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20150306-224">[221]</a></sup></li>\n<li>January 17, 2006: Launch delayed. First day launch attempts scrubbed because of unacceptable weather conditions (high winds).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes.com_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes.com-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Space.com_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-Space.com-80">[80]</a></sup></li>\n<li>January 18, 2006: Launch delayed again. Second launch attempt scrubbed because of morning power outage at the <a href="/wiki/Applied_Physics_Laboratory" title="Applied Physics Laboratory">Applied Physics Laboratory</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225"><a href="#cite_note-225">[222]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Launch_phase">Launch phase</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=57" title="Edit section: Launch phase">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<ul><li>January 19, 2006: Successful launch at 19:00 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UTC" title="UTC">UTC</a> after a brief delay due to cloud cover.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Amir_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-Amir-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harwood_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-Harwood-82">[82]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Jupiter_pre-encounter_phase">Jupiter pre-encounter phase</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=58" title="Edit section: Jupiter pre-encounter phase">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<ul><li>April 7, 2006: The probe passed <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a>\' orbit: 1.7 <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">AU</a> from Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Malik_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-Malik-93">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[223]</a></sup></li>\n<li>June 13, 2006: Flyby of asteroid <a href="/wiki/132524_APL" title="132524 APL">132524 APL</a>. The probe passed closest to the asteroid 132524 APL in the Belt at about 101,867&#160;km at 04:05&#160;UTC. Pictures were taken.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[224]</a></sup></li>\n<li>November 28, 2006: First image of Pluto. The image of Pluto was taken from a great distance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LORRI_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-LORRI-99">[99]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Jupiter_encounter_phase">Jupiter encounter phase</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=59" title="Edit section: Jupiter encounter phase">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<ul><li>January 10, 2007: Navigation exercise near <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>. Long-distance observations of Jupiter\'s outer moon <a href="/wiki/Callirrhoe_(moon)" title="Callirrhoe (moon)">Callirrhoe</a> as a navigation exercise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-callirrhoe_228-0"><a href="#cite_note-callirrhoe-228">[225]</a></sup></li>\n<li>February 28, 2007: Jupiter flyby. Closest approach occurred at 05:43:40&#160;UTC at 2.305&#160;million km, 21.219&#160;km/s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Timeline_229-0"><a href="#cite_note-Timeline-229">[226]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pluto_pre-encounter_phase">Pluto pre-encounter phase</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=60" title="Edit section: Pluto pre-encounter phase">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<ul><li>June 8, 2008: The probe passed Saturn\'s orbit: 9.5 AU from Earth. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Timeline_229-1"><a href="#cite_note-Timeline-229">[226]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[227]</a></sup></li>\n<li>December 29, 2009: The probe becomes closer to Pluto than to Earth. Pluto was then 32.7 AU from Earth, and the probe was 16.4 AU from Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[228]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232"><a href="#cite_note-232">[229]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-233"><a href="#cite_note-233">[230]</a></sup></li>\n<li>February 25, 2010: New Horizons completed 2.38&#160;billion&#160;km (1.48&#160;billion&#160;mi), half the total travel distance of 4.76&#160;billion&#160;km (2.96&#160;billion&#160;mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-234"><a href="#cite_note-234">[231]</a></sup></li>\n<li>March 18, 2011: The probe passes <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a>\'s orbit. This is the fourth planetary orbit the spacecraft crossed since its start. <i>New Horizons</i> reached Uranus\'s orbit at 22:00&#160;UTC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-235"><a href="#cite_note-235">[232]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[233]</a></sup></li>\n<li>December 2, 2011: <i>New Horizons</i> draws closer to Pluto than any other spacecraft has ever been. Previously, <i><a href="/wiki/Voyager_1" title="Voyager 1">Voyager 1</a></i> held the record for the closest approach. (~10.58 AU)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-237"><a href="#cite_note-237">[234]</a></sup></li>\n<li>February 11, 2012: <i>New Horizons</i> reaches the distance of 10 AU from the Pluto system, at around 4:55&#160;UTC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-news20120210_238-0"><a href="#cite_note-news20120210-238">[235]</a></sup></li>\n<li>July 1, 2013: <i>New Horizons</i> captures its first image of <a href="/wiki/Charon_(moon)" title="Charon (moon)">Charon</a>. Charon is clearly separated from Pluto using the Long-Range Reconnaissance Imager (LORRI).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-news20130711_239-0"><a href="#cite_note-news20130711-239">[236]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-240"><a href="#cite_note-240">[237]</a></sup></li>\n<li>October 25, 2013: <i>New Horizons</i> reaches the distance of 5 AU from the Pluto system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-news20120210_238-1"><a href="#cite_note-news20120210-238">[235]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-241"><a href="#cite_note-241">[238]</a></sup></li>\n<li>July 20, 2014: Photos of Pluto and Charon. Images obtained showing both bodies orbiting each other, distance 2.8 AU.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-242"><a href="#cite_note-242">[239]</a></sup></li>\n<li>August 25, 2014: The probe passes <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a>\'s orbit. This was the fifth planetary orbit crossed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-243"><a href="#cite_note-243">[240]</a></sup></li>\n<li>December 7, 2014: <i>New Horizons</i> awakes from hibernation. NASA\'s Deep Sky Network station at Tidbinbilla, Australia received a signal confirming that it successfully awoke from hibernation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-awoketwitter_122-1"><a href="#cite_note-awoketwitter-122">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-awokeAST_123-1"><a href="#cite_note-awokeAST-123">[123]</a></sup></li>\n<li>January 2015: Observation of <a href="/wiki/Kuiper_belt" title="Kuiper belt">Kuiper belt</a> object <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/2011_KW48" title="2011 KW48">2011 KW<span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;"><span style="font-size:80%;">48</span></span></a></span>. Distant observations from a distance of roughly 75 million km (~0.5 AU)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VNH0004_244-0"><a href="#cite_note-VNH0004-244">[241]</a></sup></li>\n<li>January 15, 2015: Start of Pluto observations. <i>New Horizons</i> is now close enough to Pluto and begins observing the system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20150115_245-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20150115-245">[242]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-246"><a href="#cite_note-246">[243]</a></sup></li>\n<li>March 10&#8211;11, 2015: <i>New Horizons</i> reaches the distance of 1 AU from the Pluto system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-247"><a href="#cite_note-247">[244]</a></sup></li>\n<li>March 20, 2015: NASA invites the general public to suggest names to surface features that will be discovered on Pluto and Charon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-248"><a href="#cite_note-248">[245]</a></sup></li>\n<li>May 15, 2015: Images exceed best <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble Space Telescope</a> resolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seeplutonow.com_249-0"><a href="#cite_note-seeplutonow.com-249">[246]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pluto_Science_phase">Pluto Science phase</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=61" title="Edit section: Pluto Science phase">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<ul><li>July 14, 2015: Flyby of the Pluto system: <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charon_(moon)" title="Charon (moon)">Charon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydra_(moon)" title="Hydra (moon)">Hydra</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nix_(moon)" title="Nix (moon)">Nix</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kerberos_(moon)" title="Kerberos (moon)">Kerberos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Styx_(moon)" title="Styx (moon)">Styx</a>. <br/>&#160;&#8226;  Flyby of Pluto around 11:49:57&#160;UTC at 12,500&#160;km, 13.78&#160;km/s. <br/>&#160;&#8226;  Pluto is 32.9 AU from Sun. <br/>&#160;&#8226;  Flyby of Charon around 12:03:50&#160;UTC at 28,858&#160;km, 13.87&#160;km/s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-presskit_250-0"><a href="#cite_note-presskit-250">[247]</a></sup></li>\n<li>July 2015 to October 2016: Transmission of collected data back to Earth, and ongoing science discovery based on the observations. The <a href="/wiki/Bit_rate" title="Bit rate">bit rate</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Downlink" title="Downlink">downlink</a> is limited to 1&#8211;2 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Data_rate_units#Kilobit_per_second" title="Data rate units">kb/s</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jhuhub20150717_147-1"><a href="#cite_note-jhuhub20150717-147">[147]</a></sup> so it took until October 25, 2016, to transmit all of the data.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20161028_19-4"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20161028-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-251"><a href="#cite_note-251">[248]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-252"><a href="#cite_note-252">[249]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-253"><a href="#cite_note-253">[250]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h3><span id=".28486958.29_2014_MU69_pre-encounter_phase"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="(486958)_2014_MU69_pre-encounter_phase"><span class="nowrap">(486958) 2014 MU<sub>69</sub></span> pre-encounter phase</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=62" title="Edit section: (486958) 2014 MU69 pre-encounter phase">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<ul><li>October 22 &#8211; November 4, 2015: Trajectory correction maneuver. Course adjustment towards the January 2019 flyby of <span class="nowrap"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2014_MU69" title="2014 MU69">2014 MU<span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;"><span style="font-size:80%;">69</span></span></a></span> was performed in a series of four thruster firings of 22 minutes each.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wired-20151104_175-1"><a href="#cite_note-wired-20151104-175">[175]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nsf-20170528_254-0"><a href="#cite_note-nsf-20170528-254">[251]</a></sup></li>\n<li>November 2, 2015: Observation of KBO <a href="/wiki/15810_Arawn" title="15810 Arawn">15810 Arawn</a>. Long-range observations from a distance of 170&#160;million miles (1.8&#160;AU), the closest ever for any Trans-Neptunian Object other than Pluto. More images were taken on April 7&#8211;8, 2016, at a range of 111&#160;million miles (1.19&#160;AU) as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-255"><a href="#cite_note-255">[252]</a></sup></li>\n<li>July 13&#8211;14, 2016: Observation of KBO <a href="/wiki/50000_Quaoar" title="50000 Quaoar">50000 Quaoar</a>. Long-range observations from a distance of 1.3&#160;billion miles (14&#160;AU) gives mission scientists a different perspective in order to study the light-scattering properties of Quaoar\'s surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-256"><a href="#cite_note-256">[253]</a></sup></li>\n<li>February 1, 2017: Trajectory correction maneuver. A small course adjustment towards the January 2019 flyby of <span class="nowrap">2014 MU<sub>69</sub></span> was performed with a 44-second thruster firing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-257"><a href="#cite_note-257">[254]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wired-20151104_175-2"><a href="#cite_note-wired-20151104-175">[175]</a></sup></li>\n<li>2017&#8211;2020: Observations of Kuiper belt objects (KBOs). The probe will have opportunities to perform observations of 10 to 20 KBOs visible from the spacecraft\'s trajectory after the Pluto system flyby. Heliosphere data collection is expected to begin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lakdawalla20150901_190-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lakdawalla20150901-190">[190]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stern-2015-08_258-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stern-2015-08-258">[255]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-259"><a href="#cite_note-259">[256]</a></sup></li>\n<li>December 9, 2017: Trajectory correction maneuver. This delays the arrival at <span class="nowrap">2014 MU<sub>69</sub></span> by a few hours, optimizing coverage by ground-based radio telescopes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lakdawalla_2018-01-24_260-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lakdawalla_2018-01-24-260">[257]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hibernation2017_261-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hibernation2017-261">[258]</a></sup></li>\n<li>December 23, 2017 &#8211; June 4, 2018: Final hibernation period before the (KBO) <span class="nowrap">2014 MU<sub>69</sub></span> encounter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-262"><a href="#cite_note-262">[259]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lakdawalla_2018-01-24_260-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lakdawalla_2018-01-24-260">[257]</a></sup></li>\n<li>August 2018 &#8211; March 2019: Distant observations of at least a dozen distant KBOs. Recovered by <a href="/wiki/Subaru_Telescope" title="Subaru Telescope">Subaru Telescope</a> in 2014&#8211;2017, enabling <i>New Horizons</i> observations<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Porter2017_179-1"><a href="#cite_note-Porter2017-179">[179]</a></sup></li>\n<li>August 13, 2018: Switch from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spin-stabilized_satellite" title="Spin-stabilized satellite">spin mode</a> to 3-axis mode.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lakdawalla_2018-01-24_260-2"><a href="#cite_note-Lakdawalla_2018-01-24-260">[257]</a></sup></li>\n<li>August 16, 2018 &#8211; December 24, 2018: Approach phase. Optical navigation, search for hazardous material around <span class="nowrap">2014 MU<sub>69</sub></span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lakdawalla_2018-01-24_260-3"><a href="#cite_note-Lakdawalla_2018-01-24-260">[257]</a></sup></li>\n<li>August 16, 2018: First detection of Kuiper belt object <span class="nowrap">2014 MU<sub>69</sub></span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-First_detection_199-1"><a href="#cite_note-First_detection-199">[198]</a></sup></li>\n<li>October 3, 2018 &#8211; December 2, 2018: Opportunities for trajectory correction maneuvers. Maneuvers scheduled for October 3 and November 20, with backups on October 23 and December 2, respectively<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lakdawalla_2018-01-24_260-4"><a href="#cite_note-Lakdawalla_2018-01-24-260">[257]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2014_MU69_Science_phase_and_beyond"><span class="nowrap">2014 MU<sub>69</sub></span> Science phase and beyond</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=63" title="Edit section: 2014 MU69 Science phase and beyond">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<ul><li>January 1, 2019: Flyby of Kuiper belt object <span class="nowrap">2014 MU<sub>69</sub></span>. The flyby occurred at 05:33&#160;<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UTC" title="UTC">UTC</a>, and is the outermost close encounter of any Solar System object.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sfnow20180106_202-1"><a href="#cite_note-sfnow20180106-202">[201]</a></sup></li>\n<li>January 9, 2019: Switch from 3-axis mode to spin mode. This ends the <span class="nowrap">2014 MU<sub>69</sub></span> flyby, marking the beginning of the downlink phase.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lakdawalla_2018-01-24_260-5"><a href="#cite_note-Lakdawalla_2018-01-24-260">[257]</a></sup></li>\n<li>2019&#8211;2020: Downlink of data from <span class="nowrap">2014 MU<sub>69</sub></span> flyby. Predicted to take approximately 18 months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lakdawalla_2018-01-24_260-6"><a href="#cite_note-Lakdawalla_2018-01-24-260">[257]</a></sup></li>\n<li>April 30, 2021: End of extended mission. It is expected that the mission will be extended further if the spacecraft remains operational.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lakdawalla_2018-01-24_260-7"><a href="#cite_note-Lakdawalla_2018-01-24-260">[257]</a></sup></li>\n<li>2020s: The probe may be able to fly by a third KBO. The probe approached <span class="nowrap">2014 MU<sub>69</sub></span> along its rotational axis, which simplified trajectory correction maneuvers, saving fuel that could be used to target another KBO.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-263"><a href="#cite_note-263">[260]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-space_122118_264-0"><a href="#cite_note-space_122118-264">[261]</a></sup> After the flyby, the spacecraft was left with 11&#160;kg (24&#160;lb) of fuel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-265"><a href="#cite_note-265">[262]</a></sup></li>\n<li>Mid to late 2030s: Expected end of the mission, based on <a href="/wiki/Radioisotope_thermoelectric_generator" title="Radioisotope thermoelectric generator">RTG</a> decay. Heliosphere data collection expected to be intermittent if instrument power sharing is required.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-end_of_mission_266-0"><a href="#cite_note-end_of_mission-266">[263]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-space_122118_264-1"><a href="#cite_note-space_122118-264">[261]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-mission_phase">Post-mission phase</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=64" title="Edit section: Post-mission phase">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<ul><li>2038: <i>New Horizons</i> will be 100 AU from the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>. If still functioning, the probe will explore the outer <a href="/wiki/Heliosphere" title="Heliosphere">heliosphere</a> along with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voyager_spacecraft" title="Voyager spacecraft">Voyager spacecraft</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jhuapl2006_211-1"><a href="#cite_note-jhuapl2006-211">[209]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=65" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="div-col columns column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: mission</li>\n<li><i><a href="/wiki/Pioneer_10" title="Pioneer 10">Pioneer 10</a></i></li>\n<li><i><a href="/wiki/Pioneer_11" title="Pioneer 11">Pioneer 11</a></i></li>\n<li><i><a 2x" width="24"/></a></span><a href="/wiki/Portal:Ganymede_(moon)" title="Portal:Ganymede (moon)">Ganymede (moon) portal</a></span></li></ul></div>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=66" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">\n<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-note-195"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-note_195-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Confirmation that <i>New Horizons</i> exited hibernation was received by ground stations at 06:12&#160;UTC. <a href="/wiki/Spacecraft_Event_Time" title="Spacecraft Event Time">Spacecraft Event Time</a> is calculated by subtracting the one-way light-travel time (5 hours, 38 minutes, 38 seconds) from Earth-received time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-position20180606_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-position20180606-194">[194]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-210"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-210">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">To escape the Sun the spacecraft needs a speed relative to the Sun of the square root of 2 times the speed of the Earth (29.78&#160;km/s), or 42.1&#160;km/s. Relative to the Earth this is just 12.3&#160;km/s. But the kinetic energy when near the surface of the Earth must include the energy to exit the gravity well of the Earth, which requires a speed of about 11 km/s. The total speed needed is the square root of the sum of the squares of these two speeds.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-parker-note-212"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-parker-note_212-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-parker-note_212-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">The <i>Parker Solar Probe</i> is expected to beat this record at its next <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perihelion" title="Perihelion">perihelion</a> in April 2019. Following several more <a href="/wiki/Gravity_assist" title="Gravity assist">gravity assists</a> at Venus, the spacecraft is expected to reach a maximum speed at perihelion of approximately 200&#160;km/s (720,000&#160;km/h; 450,000&#160;mph) on December 24, 2024.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hollingum_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hollingum-215">[212]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n</ol></div></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Horizons&amp;action=edit&amp;section=67" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">\n<ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="http://www.ilslaunch.com/atlas-launch-archives" rel="nofollow">"Atlas Launch Archives"</a>.
